Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The First Six Necessary Divine Attributes
Sanusi lists the first six necessary divine attributes with brief definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first six of the twenty attributes that reason necessarily affirms for God, as enumerated by Sanusi.

Core Idea
The first six attributes establish God's existence, eternality, and absolute uniqueness.

Sanusi begins his enumeration of the twenty attributes that reason alone shows must belong to God. In this passage, he lists the first six, which establish God's existence, eternity, self-subsistence, and absolute uniqueness. Understanding these is the starting point for all later theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise listing style and the brief definitions embedded within the sentence.
The passage ends with a question mark in the OCR; it likely continues on the next page.
2
Identify each attribute
List the six attributes mentioned: existence (al-wujud), eternity without beginning (al-qidam), perpetuity (al-baqa'), being unlike originated things (mukhalafatu al-hawadith), self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi), and oneness (al-wahdaniyyah).
3
Understand the definitions
Sanusi gives short clarifications for self-subsistence (no need for location or specifier) and oneness (no second in essence, attributes, or actions). Reflect on what these imply about God.
These definitions are crucial for avoiding misconceptions.
4
Reflect on rational necessity
These attributes are not derived from scripture alone but from rational necessity. Ask yourself: why must God have these attributes?
This is a key principle in kalam – reason independently affirms these.
5
Connect to the larger list
Keep in mind that this is only the beginning; the full set of twenty attributes continues on the following pages. These first six form the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمِمّا يجبُ لِمَوْلانا جل وعزّ عِشرونَ صِفَةٌ وَهِيَ : الوُجُودُ، وَالقِدَمُ، والبقاءُ، وَمخالفَتُهُ تَعَالَى للحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، أي: لا يَفْتَقِرُ إلى محلّ ولا مُخصّص، وَالوَحدَانِيَّةُ :أي: لَا ثَانِي لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا في أفعاله؟
Then among that which is necessary for our Lord, Mighty and Majestic, are twenty attributes, and they are: existence (al-wujud), eternity without beginning (al-qidam), perpetuity (al-baqa’), being unlike originated things (mukhalafatuhu ta‘ala lil-hawadith), being self-subsisting (qiyamuhu ta‘ala bi-nafsihi), i.e., He does not depend upon a location or a specifier, and oneness (al-wahdaniyyah), i.e., there is no second to Him in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions?
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage begins the enumeration of the twenty attributes that are rationally necessary for God. Sanusi states that among the things wajib (necessary) for God are twenty attributes, and he lists the first few: 1) Existence (al-wujud) – that God exists. 2) Eternity without beginning (al-qidam) – God has no beginning. 3) Perpetuity (al-baqa’) – He has no end. 4) Being unlike originated things (mukhalafatuhu lil-hawadith) – God is not similar to any created being. 5) Self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi) – He does not need a location or a specifier (i.e., He does not depend on anything else for His existence). 6) Oneness (al-wahdaniyyah) – He is one in essence, attributes, and actions; there is no second to Him in any respect. The list is cut off at this point, but the next page would continue with the remaining attributes. Each attribute is defined briefly.

Key Terms
الصفات الواجبة
الوجود
القدم
البقاء
مخالفة الحوادث
قيامه بنفسه
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think these attributes are only known from revelation, not reason.
Correction: Sanusi presents them as rationally necessary, not merely derived from scripture. Reason itself demands them.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed among the divine attributes in this passage?
A) Existence (al-wujud).
B) Oneness (al-wahdaniyyah).
C) Knowledge (al-‘ilm).
D) Eternity without beginning (al-qidam).

Key Takeaway
The first six necessary attributes establish God's existence, eternity, self-subsistence, and oneness as rationally demanded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God is unlike anything you have ever seen or imagined? How does that change the way you picture Him in your mind?
?
If God is self-subsisting and does not depend on anything, where do you place your ultimate trust? Is it in Him alone, or do you still rely on created things as if they were independent?
?
The oneness of God extends to His attributes and actions: do you ever attribute power or success to other than God, as if there were a 'second' in your life? What would it mean to align your heart fully with His oneness?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Twenty Attributes and the Nafsiyya Attribute
Sanusi enumerates the twenty attributes and classifies the first six, highlighting existence as nafsiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twenty necessary attributes of Allah and the classification of the first six, with a focus on existence as the nafsiyya (essential) attribute.

Core Idea
Existence is the only essential attribute; the other five are relational.

Sanusi begins by listing the twenty attributes incumbent upon Allah. He then singles out the first attribute—existence—as nafsiyya, meaning it is not a positive attribute added to the essence but rather the essence itself. This lesson orients you to the full list and clarifies the unique nature of the essential attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the flow from the general list to the specific classification.
The passage lists all twenty attributes in the opening sentence.
2
Identify the six attributes
Sanusi says 'فهذِهِ سِتُّ صِفَاتٍ' (so these are six attributes). Identify which six he means: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, unlike created things, self-subsistence, and oneness.
Existence is the first and is nafsiyya; the other five are often called salbiyya (negative).
3
Understand nafsiyya
Existence is called nafsiyya because it is the essence itself, not an added attribute. Contrast with the five that follow.
The commentary explains that nafsiyya means not a positive existential attribute.
4
Reflect on the remaining attributes
The passage implies that the twenty include more than these six; the next lessons will cover the other fourteen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمِمّا يجبُ لِمَوْلانَا جل وعزّ عِشرونَ صِفَةٌ وَهِيَ : الوُجُودُ، وَالقِدَمُ، والبقاءُ، وَمخالفَتُهُ تَعَالَى للحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، أي: لا يَفْتَقِرُ إلى محلّ ولا مُخصّص، وَالوَحدَانِيَّةُ :أي: لَا ثَانِي لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا في أفعاله. فهذِهِ سِتُّ صِفَاتٍ: الأولى نَفْسِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ الوُجُودُ، والخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعالى سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمَّى : صِفَات المعَانِي وهِيَ : القُدْرَةُ والإرَادَةُ المتَعَلِقتَان بِجَمِيعِ المُمكنات، والعِلْمُ المتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائِزَاتِ والمُسْتحِيلات. وَالحياةُ وَهِيَ لا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشيءٍ. والسَّمْعُ وَالبصرُ المتعلِّقانِ بِجَميعِ المَوْجُودَاتِ. والكلامُ الَّذِي ليسَ بِحرْفِ وَلَا صوتٍ وَيَتعلّقُ بِما يتعلق بِهِ العِلمُ مِنَ المُتَعلقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمّى: صفاتٍ مَعنَوِيَّةٌ، وهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الأولى، وهِيَ كُونُهُ تَعالى قَادِراً، ومُرِيداً وعَالِماً، وحيّاً، وسَمِيعاً، وبَصِيراً، ومُتَكَلّماً.
Among what is obligatory for our Lord, majestic and exalted, are twenty attributes: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, His unlike created things, His self-subsistence, meaning He does not need a locus nor a specifyer, and oneness, meaning there is no second to Him in His essence nor in His attributes nor in His actions. So these are six attributes: the first is essential, and it is existence, and the five after it [then it is obligatory for Him, exalted, seven attributes called attributes of meanings: they are: power and will, both of which pertain to all possible things; knowledge, which pertains to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things; life, which does not pertain to anything; hearing and sight, which pertain to all existent things; and speech, which is not a letter or sound and pertains to the same things as knowledge pertains to. Then seven attributes called meaning-related attributes, which are inseparable from the first seven: that He is powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage classifies and enumerates the twenty necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) of Allah. Sanusi first lists all twenty: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, self-subsistence, oneness, then the seven attributes of meanings (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech), and finally the seven meaning-related attributes (being powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking). He then explains the grouping: the first attribute, existence, is called nafsiyya (essential) because it is not a positive existential attribute added to the essence; the next five are not classified here but are often called salbiyya (negative/relational). The seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are real existential attributes that inhere in the essence, each with its own object of relation (ta'allaq). The seven meaning-related attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) are the ascriptions that necessarily follow from possessing the seven attributes of meanings; they are not additional existents but the state of being described by those attributes.

Key Terms
صفت نَفْسِيَّة (essential attribute)
الوُجُود (existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is an attribute added to Allah's essence like the other attributes.
Correction: Existence is the nafsiyya attribute, meaning it is the essence itself, not an added existential attribute.

Checkpoint
How many attributes does Sanusi list in the opening sentence of this passage?
A) 6
B) 20
C) 7
D) 14

Key Takeaway
The first of the twenty attributes, existence, is the nafsiyya (essential) attribute; the five that follow are not essential but relational or negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence is not an added attribute but His very essence, how should that reshape the way you rely on Him daily? In moments of fear or doubt, do you act as if His existence is the most certain reality, or do you treat it as a distant belief? What would change if you internalized this truth?
?
The source teaches that existence alone is essential to Allah; the other attributes describe how He differs from creation. Are there ways you unconsciously imagine Allah with human-like limitations? How does this lesson compel you to purify your conception of Him in your worship and thoughts?
?
Sanusi states that Allah's oneness (الوحدانية) means no second in essence, attributes, or actions. Does your life reflect that oneness? Where do you still place 'seconds'—people, goals, or comforts—that compete with Allah in your heart, even if you intellectually affirm His uniqueness?

Divine Attributes

The Seven Attributes of Meanings
Sanusi enumerates the seven attributes of meanings with their specific objects of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) and how each relates to its object (ta'allaq).

Core Idea
The seven attributes of meanings are real existential attributes, each with a specific scope.

After listing the first six attributes, Sanusi proceeds to the seven attributes called 'sifat al-ma'ani' (attributes of meanings). These are real, positive attributes that inhere in Allah's essence. Each has a distinct object of relation (ta'allaq) that defines what it encompasses. This lesson walks through each one and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the term 'صفات المعاني' and the list that follows.
These attributes are distinct from the first six.
2
Enumerate the seven
Identify each: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech.
Each has a unique ta'allaq.
3
Understand the ta'allaq
Power and will pertain to all possible things; knowledge to all categories; life to nothing; hearing and sight to all existents; speech to the same as knowledge, but not a letter or sound.
The ta'allaq shows the scope of each attribute.
4
Reflect on speech
Speech is defined negatively: not a letter or sound. This distinguishes it from human speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمِمّا يجبُ لِمَوْلانَا جل وعزّ عِشرونَ صِفَةٌ وَهِيَ : الوُجُودُ، وَالقِدَمُ، والبقاءُ، وَمخالفَتُهُ تَعَالَى للحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، أي: لا يَفْتَقِرُ إلى محلّ ولا مُخصّص، وَالوَحدَانِيَّةُ :أي: لَا ثَانِي لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا في أفعاله. فهذِهِ سِتُّ صِفَاتٍ: الأولى نَفْسِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ الوُجُودُ، والخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعالى سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمَّى : صِفَات المعَانِي وهِيَ : القُدْرَةُ والإرَادَةُ المتَعَلِقتَان بِجَمِيعِ المُمكنات، والعِلْمُ المتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائِزَاتِ والمُسْتحِيلات. وَالحياةُ وَهِيَ لا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشيءٍ. والسَّمْعُ وَالبصرُ المتعلِّقانِ بِجَميعِ المَوْجُودَاتِ. والكلامُ الَّذِي ليسَ بِحرْفِ وَلَا صوتٍ وَيَتعلّقُ بِما يتعلق بِهِ العِلمُ مِنَ المُتَعلقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمّى: صفاتٍ مَعنَوِيَّةٌ، وهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الأولى، وهِيَ كُونُهُ تَعالى قَادِراً، ومُرِيداً وعَالِماً، وحيّاً، وسَمِيعاً، وبَصِيراً، ومُتَكَلّماً.
Among what is obligatory for our Lord, majestic and exalted, are twenty attributes: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, His unlike created things, His self-subsistence, meaning He does not need a locus nor a specifyer, and oneness, meaning there is no second to Him in His essence nor in His attributes nor in His actions. So these are six attributes: the first is essential, and it is existence, and the five after it [then it is obligatory for Him, exalted, seven attributes called attributes of meanings: they are: power and will, both of which pertain to all possible things; knowledge, which pertains to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things; life, which does not pertain to anything; hearing and sight, which pertain to all existent things; and speech, which is not a letter or sound and pertains to the same things as knowledge pertains to. Then seven attributes called meaning-related attributes, which are inseparable from the first seven: that He is powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage classifies and enumerates the twenty necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) of Allah. Sanusi first lists all twenty: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, self-subsistence, oneness, then the seven attributes of meanings (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech), and finally the seven meaning-related attributes (being powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking). He then explains the grouping: the first attribute, existence, is called nafsiyya (essential) because it is not a positive existential attribute added to the essence; the next five are not classified here but are often called salbiyya (negative/relational). The seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are real existential attributes that inhere in the essence, each with its own object of relation (ta'allaq). The seven meaning-related attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) are the ascriptions that necessarily follow from possessing the seven attributes of meanings; they are not additional existents but the state of being described by those attributes.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني (attributes of meanings)
تَعَلُّق (object of relation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attributes of meanings are separate from Allah's essence, like independent entities.
Correction: They are real existential attributes that inhere in the essence, not separate entities.

Checkpoint
How many attributes of meanings does Sanusi list?
A) Six
B) Seven
C) Eight
D) Twenty

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meanings are real attributes with distinct scopes, from power over possibilities to knowledge of all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge encompasses even your most secret thoughts, what hidden habit or assumption about yourself needs to change because you cannot hide from Him? Will you confess it to Him today and seek His help to transform it?
?
Life, as a precondition for all other actions, is given to you moment by moment. Are you using your time as if you believe it is a loan that will be called back? What will you do differently today to ensure you are not wasting this gift?

Divine Attributes

The Seven Meaning-Related Attributes
Sanusi enumerates the seven meaning-related attributes as necessary concomitants of the attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven meaning-related attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) and how they are necessary concomitants of the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Meaning-related attributes are not additional existences but necessary ascriptions.

Sanusi completes his classification by introducing the seven meaning-related attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya). These are not additional existential attributes; rather, they are the necessary descriptions that follow from possessing the seven attributes of meanings. This lesson clarifies their nature and relationship to the previous group.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the term 'صفات معنوية' and the list.
These are the last seven of the twenty.
2
Enumerate the seven
They are: powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking. Each corresponds to one of the seven attributes of meanings.
These are not additional attributes but the state of being described by the first seven.
3
Understand 'inseparable'
Sanusi says they are 'ملازمة للسبع الأولى' (inseparable from the first seven). This means they necessarily exist because the attributes of meanings exist.
They are not separate existents; they are concomitants.
4
Distinguish from ma'ani
The attributes of meanings are existential attributes in the essence; the meaning-related attributes are the ascriptions that result from them.
For example, if Allah has power (attribute of meaning), He is powerful (meaning-related).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمِمّا يجبُ لِمَوْلانَا جل وعزّ عِشرونَ صِفَةٌ وَهِيَ : الوُجُودُ، وَالقِدَمُ، والبقاءُ، وَمخالفَتُهُ تَعَالَى للحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، أي: لا يَفْتَقِرُ إلى محلّ ولا مُخصّص، وَالوَحدَانِيَّةُ :أي: لَا ثَانِي لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا في أفعاله. فهذِهِ سِتُّ صِفَاتٍ: الأولى نَفْسِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ الوُجُودُ، والخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعالى سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمَّى : صِفَات المعَانِي وهِيَ : القُدْرَةُ والإرَادَةُ المتَعَلِقتَان بِجَمِيعِ المُمكنات، والعِلْمُ المتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائِزَاتِ والمُسْتحِيلات. وَالحياةُ وَهِيَ لا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشيءٍ. والسَّمْعُ وَالبصرُ المتعلِّقانِ بِجَميعِ المَوْجُودَاتِ. والكلامُ الَّذِي ليسَ بِحرْفِ وَلَا صوتٍ وَيَتعلّقُ بِما يتعلق بِهِ العِلمُ مِنَ المُتَعلقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمّى: صفاتٍ مَعنَوِيَّةٌ، وهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الأولى، وهِيَ كُونُهُ تَعالى قَادِراً، ومُرِيداً وعَالِماً، وحيّاً، وسَمِيعاً، وبَصِيراً، ومُتَكَلّماً.
Among what is obligatory for our Lord, majestic and exalted, are twenty attributes: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, His unlike created things, His self-subsistence, meaning He does not need a locus nor a specifyer, and oneness, meaning there is no second to Him in His essence nor in His attributes nor in His actions. So these are six attributes: the first is essential, and it is existence, and the five after it [then it is obligatory for Him, exalted, seven attributes called attributes of meanings: they are: power and will, both of which pertain to all possible things; knowledge, which pertains to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things; life, which does not pertain to anything; hearing and sight, which pertain to all existent things; and speech, which is not a letter or sound and pertains to the same things as knowledge pertains to. Then seven attributes called meaning-related attributes, which are inseparable from the first seven: that He is powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and speaking.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage classifies and enumerates the twenty necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) of Allah. Sanusi first lists all twenty: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike created things, self-subsistence, oneness, then the seven attributes of meanings (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech), and finally the seven meaning-related attributes (being powerful, willing, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, speaking). He then explains the grouping: the first attribute, existence, is called nafsiyya (essential) because it is not a positive existential attribute added to the essence; the next five are not classified here but are often called salbiyya (negative/relational). The seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are real existential attributes that inhere in the essence, each with its own object of relation (ta'allaq). The seven meaning-related attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) are the ascriptions that necessarily follow from possessing the seven attributes of meanings; they are not additional existents but the state of being described by those attributes.

Key Terms
صفات معنوية (meaning-related attributes)
مُلازِمَة (inseparable)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meaning-related attributes are additional existential attributes separate from the attributes of meanings.
Correction: They are necessary concomitants (not additional existents) that describe the state of being characterized by the attributes of meanings.

Checkpoint
How many meaning-related attributes does Sanusi list?
A) 6
B) 7
C) 14
D) 20

Key Takeaway
The seven meaning-related attributes are not additional existential attributes but necessary descriptions that follow from the seven attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly grasp that Allah is described as 'powerful' (qādiran) by necessity and not as an optional label, in what specific area of your life will you trust His power more, beginning today? Write down one situation where you have acted as if His power were limited.
?
The passage teaches that Allah is 'knowing' (āliman) inherently. Knowing that He sees through every excuse and hidden motive, what secret thought or habit are you avoiding confronting? Will you repent from it now?
?
Since Allah's being 'living' (ḥayyan) is an eternal and necessary attribute, your existence is a fleeting gift from Him. How will you use the remaining hours of this day to live in a way that acknowledges His life-giving sovereignty?

Divine Attributes

Six Necessary and Seven Attributes of Meaning
Sanusi enumerates six necessary attributes and defines the seven attributes of meaning with their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine attributes: six necessary attributes (including existence) and seven attributes of meaning with their specific relations.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are divided into necessary attributes and attributes of meaning.

Sanusi now introduces the core categories of divine attributes. First, he lists the six necessary attributes that are essential to Allah's essence, beginning with existence. Then, he presents the seven attributes of meaning—also called sifāt al-maʿānī—which are additional eternal attributes that subsist in Allah's essence and are defined by their relation to creation. This passage establishes the foundational taxonomy of attributes in Ashʿarī theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage first mentions six necessary attributes, then seven attributes of meaning. Notice that only the first necessary attribute (existence) is named; the other five are assumed known.
2
Understand the attributes of meaning
Each of the seven attributes is defined by its scope of relation (taʿalluq). Pay attention to how power and will relate to all possibles, knowledge to all things, life to nothing, hearing and sight to existents, and speech to the same as knowledge.
3
Connect to prior lessons
Recall that Allah exists necessarily. This attribute is the first among the six necessary. The attributes of meaning are additional, not identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذِهِ سِتُّ صِفَاتٍ: الأولى نَفْسِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ الوُجُودُ، والخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا. ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعالى سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمَّى : صِفَات المعَانِي وهِيَ : القُدْرَةُ والإرَادَةُ المتَعَلِقتَان بِجَمِيعِ المُمكنات، والعِلْمُ المتعلّق بجميع الواجبات والجائِزَاتِ والمُسْتحِيلات. وَالحياةُ وَهِيَ لا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشيءٍ. والسَّمْعُ وَالبصرُ المتعلِّقانِ بِجَميعِ المَوْجُودَاتِ. والكلامُ الَّذِي ليسَ بِحرْفِ وَلَا صوتٍ وَيَتعلّقُ بِما يتعلق بِهِ العِلمُ مِنَ المُتَعلقَاتِ.
So these are six attributes: the first is essential (nafsiyya), which is existence, and the five after it. Then He, exalted, must have seven attributes called the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), which are: power (qudra) and will (irāda), both of which relate to all possible things; knowledge (ʿilm) which relates to all necessary, possible, and impossible things; life (ḥayāh), which does not relate to anything; hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar), which relate to all existent things; and speech (kalām), which is neither letter nor sound, and relates to what knowledge relates to of things related.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and definition of two categories of divine attributes. First, Sanusi states that there are six attributes, the first being the essential attribute (nafsiyya), which is existence (wujūd). The other five are not named here but are understood to be pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to created things, self-subsistence, and oneness. Then he moves to the seven attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) that are necessitated for Allah. He lists them: power and will, both relating to all possible things (al-mumkināt); knowledge, relating to all necessary, possible, and impossible things; life, which does not relate to anything (it is a precondition for other attributes); hearing and sight, relating to all existent things; and speech, which is not composed of letters or sounds and has the same scope as knowledge. Each attribute is defined by its relation (taʿalluq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The list of six necessary attributes is incomplete because only existence is mentioned.
Correction: Sanusi only names the first necessary attribute (existence) but the other five are known from the Ash'ari tradition: pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity, self-subsistence, and oneness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the seven attributes of meaning (sifāt al-maʿānī) listed by Sanusi?
A) Power (qudra)
B) Will (irāda)
C) Pre-eternity (qidam)
D) Speech (kalām)

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes are categorized into six necessary attributes (the first being existence) and seven attributes of meaning (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech) each with a specific relation to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The attribute of hearing and sight relates to all existent things. If you truly believed that Allah hears every whisper and sees every hidden action today, what would you do differently right now? What holds you back from living with that awareness?
?
Allah's speech is not like human speech — it is neither letter nor sound. How does this truth challenge the way you approach the Quran? If the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah, what does that demand of your heart when you recite it?
?
Knowledge relates to all necessary, possible, and impossible things. Your life plan is in the realm of possible things. Does your trust in Allah's knowledge give you peace, or do you still worry about the future? What concrete step can you take today to entrust your affairs to the One who knows all?

The Seven Derived Attributes (Ṣifāt Maʿnawiyya)
Sanusi enumerates the seven derived attributes and states their relation to the attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the derived attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya) are and how they relate to the attributes of meaning.

Core Idea
The derived attributes are the logical consequences of the attributes of meaning.

Sanusi now introduces a second set of divine attributes intimately linked to the first. These are not new realities but rather the expression of those realities in terms of God's active descriptions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a precise theological vocabulary about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the two sets of attributes
The passage mentions the 'first seven' (attributes of meaning) and then seven 'derived attributes'. Note how each derived attribute corresponds to an attribute of meaning.
For example, power (qudra) corresponds to being powerful (qādir).
3
Understand the relationship: concomitant (mulāzima)
The derived attributes are said to be 'concomitant' with the attributes of meaning. This means wherever an attribute of meaning exists, its derived attribute necessarily follows.
They are not separate realities but one reality described differently.
4
Memorize the list of seven derived attributes
The seven are: qādir, murīd, ʿālim, ḥayy, samīʿ, baṣīr, mutakallim. Commit them to memory.
These are the names by which God describes Himself in the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفاتٍ تُسمّى: صفاتٍ مَعنَوِيَّةٌ، وهِيَ مُلازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الأولى، وهِيَ كُونُهُ تَعالى قَادِراً، ومُرِيداً وعَالِماً، وحيّاً، وسَمِيعاً، وبَصِيراً، ومُتَكَلّماً.
Then seven attributes called the derived attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya), which are concomitant with the first seven, and they are that He, exalted, is powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (ʿālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samīʿ), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a classification of another set of attributes: the derived attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya). Sanusi explains that these seven attributes are necessary concomitants (mulāzima) of the first seven attributes of meaning. While the attributes of meaning are abstract realities subsisting in God (e.g., power, will, knowledge), the derived attributes express that God is described by those realities: He is powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), knowing (ʿālim), living (ḥayy), hearing (samīʿ), seeing (baṣīr), and speaking (mutakallim). These are not additional real attributes but the ascription of the attributes of meaning to the essence.

Key Terms
معنوية
ملازمة
قادر
مريد
عالم
حي
سميع
بصير
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the derived attributes are separate and additional realities independent of the attributes of meaning.
Correction: The derived attributes are not separate; they are the same realities as the attributes of meaning but considered from the perspective of describing God by them.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between the seven attributes of meaning and the seven derived attributes?
A) They are contradictory
B) The derived attributes are concomitant with the attributes of meaning
C) The attributes of meaning are derived from the derived attributes
D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The seven derived attributes (qādir, murīd, ʿālim, ḥayy, samīʿ, baṣīr, mutakallim) are necessary concomitants of the seven attributes of meaning, not additional realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is eternally powerful (qādir), does your daily life reflect reliance on His power or on your own strength? What specific action can you take today to acknowledge His absolute power over your affairs?
?
The derived attributes show that God's essence is described by life, hearing, sight, speech. Do you live as if God sees and hears you constantly? How would your private moments change if you were fully aware that He is hearing and seeing you right now?
?
Knowing that God's attributes are necessarily concomitant with His essence, what does that imply about your relationship with Him? Are you treating His attributes as real and present in your life, or as abstract concepts? Commit to one change in your worship that reflects this reality.

The First Impossibilities: Non-existence, Origination, Termination, and Dissimilarity
Sanusi lists the first four impossible attributes and expands on the meaning of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four of the twenty impossible attributes for Allah and the detailed negation of any similarity to creation.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely unlike any created thing—no body, no place, no size, no origin.

Sanusi begins the list of impossible attributes by establishing the fundamental negations that safeguard Allah's transcendence. The first four—non-existence, origination, termination, and similarity to originated things—directly oppose the necessary attributes of existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, and dissimilitude. This lesson unpacks these core negatives and shows how similarity is ruled out in all its forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening enumeration
Start by reading the Arabic passage that introduces the twenty impossibilities and lists the first four.
Notice that these are opposites of the first four necessary attributes.
2
Identify the four impossibilities
Make a list: ʿadam, ḥudūth, ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam, and mumāthala.
These correspond to existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, and dissimilarity.
3
Examine the negation of similarity
Read the detailed breakdown of what similarity would entail: being a body, accident, having direction, etc.
Each item is a way a created being is limited; Allah is free from all.
4
Reflect on transcendence
Consider how negating each of these preserves Allah's uniqueness and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِمَّا يَستَحِيلُ في حقهِ تَعالى عِشرونَ صِفةٌ، وَهِي أَضْدَادُ العِشْرِينَ الأُولى. وَهِيَ: العَدَمُ، والحدُوثُ، وَطرُوء العَدَمِ، والمماثَلةُ لِلحَوادِثِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ جِرْماً، أي: تأخُذُ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّةُ قَدْراً مِنَ الفَرَاغ، أوْ يَكُونَ عَرَضاً يَقُومُ بِالجِرْمِ، أو يكُونَ فِي جهةٍ لِلجِرْمِ، أو لهُ هُوَ جِهَةٌ، أو يَتَقَيَّدَ بِمَكَانٍ أو زَمَانٍ، أو تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّة بَالحَوادِثِ، أو يَتَّصِفَ بِالصَّغَرِ، أو الكِبَر، أو يَتَّصفَ بالأغراض في الأفعالِ والأحكام. وَكَذَا يَسْتَجِيلُ عَليهِ تَعَالَى أَنْ لا يَكُونَ قائِماً بِنَفْسِهِ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفةٌ يَقُومُ بِمَحلّ، أَوْ يَحتَاجَ إِلى مُخَصِّص. وكذَا يَسْتَحيلُ عَليهِ تَعالى أَنْ لا يَكونَ واحداً، بِأَنْ يَكونَ مُرَكَّباً فِي ذَاتِهِ، أو يكُونَ لَهُ مُماثل في ذاته، أو في صِفاتِهِ، أو يَكُونَ مَعهُ في الوُجودِ مؤثر في فعل منَ الأَفْعالِ. وكَذا يَستَحيلُ عَليهِ تعالى العَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِنٍ ما، وإيجاد شيء من العالم مَعَ كَراهته لِوُجودِهِ، أي: عَدمِ إرادته لهُ تَعالى، أو مَعَ النُّهولِ، أو الغفلة، أو بالتغليل، أو بالطبع. وكذا يستحيل عليهِ تعالى الجَهْل وَمَا فَي مَعْناهُ بِمَعْلُومٍ مَا. وَالمَوْتُ، وَالصَّممُ، والعَمَى، والبكم. وأضْدَادُ الصِّفاتِ المَعْنويَّةِ واضِحَةٌ مِنْ هذِهِ.
And from what is impossible in His right, exalted, are twenty attributes, and they are the opposites of the first twenty. They are: non-existence (ʿadam), origination (ḥudūth), termination (ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam), similarity to originated things (mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith) by being a body (jirm), meaning that His exalted essence occupies a measure of space, or being an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in a body, or being in a direction (jiha) relative to a body, or having a direction Himself, or being restricted by place or time, or His exalted essence being characterized by originated accidents (ḥawādith), or being characterized by smallness (ṣighar) or bigness (kibar), or being characterized by purposes (aġrāḍ) in actions and judgments. Likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be self-subsistent (qāʾim bi nafsih), such as being an attribute subsisting in a locus, or needing a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be one (wāḥid), such as being composite (murakkab) in His essence, or having a similar being in essence or attributes, or having with Him in existence an effecter in any action. And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, incapability (ʿajz) of any possible thing, and bringing into existence something from the world while disliking its existence (i.e., not willing it, exalted), or with repugnance (nuhūl), or heedlessness (ghafla), or by compulsion (taghlīl?), or by nature (ṭabʿ). And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, ignorance (jahl) and what is in its meaning regarding anything known. And death (mawt), deafness (ṣamam), blindness (ʿamā), and muteness (bakam). And the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the twenty impossible attributes for Allah, which are the opposites of the twenty necessary attributes (the six essential + seven of meaning + seven derived). Sanusi lists them with explanations. The first four are: non-existence, origination, termination, and similarity to originated things. He then explains that similarity includes being a body (occupying space), being an accident, having a direction, being restricted by place/time, being subject to accidents, having size (small/big), and having purposes. Then he adds: not being self-subsistent (i.e., needing a locus or specificator), not being one (i.e., composition, having a peer, or sharing influence), incapability, creating while disliking or without will (heedlessness, compulsion, nature), ignorance, and then death, deafness, blindness, muteness. He concludes that the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these (e.g., not being powerful follows from incapability).

عدم
حدوث
طرؤ العدم
مماثلة
جرم
عرض
جهة
صغر
كبر
أغراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is like a human or other created being in some way, e.g., having a body or being in a direction.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly negates all forms of similarity, including body, accident, direction, size, and purposes, affirming complete dissimilarity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is included in Sanusi's explanation of similarity to originated things?
A) Being composed of parts
B) Being a body occupying space
C) Being eternal
D) Being omnipotent

Key Takeaway
To truly affirm Allah's transcendence, we must negate every form of similarity to creation—no body, no accident, no direction, no place, no time, no size, and no purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you close your eyes to imagine Allah, do you unconsciously picture a form or location? How does this passage call you to strip away every mental image and submit to absolute transcendence?
?
You say 'Allah is with me'—have you ever meant it in a spatial sense (like His being inside the room)? How must you now correct that thought, knowing that direction and place are impossible for Him?
?
Think of a recent difficulty where you felt Allah should have acted for a clear purpose you could understand. According to this passage, having purposes (aġrāḍ) is a form of similarity. Will you trust that His actions transcend human reasoning, even when it hurts?

Negating Dependence and Composition: Self-Subsistence and Oneness
Sanusi states the impossibility of not being self-subsistent and not being one, with explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah must be self-subsistent and one, and how negation of opposites affirms His independence and uniqueness.

Core Idea
Allah needs no support and has no partner or parts.

After establishing the fundamental impossibilities, Sanusi moves to two essential attributes: being self-subsistent (qāʾim bi nafsih) and being one (wāḥid). Their opposites—dependency and composition—are impossible. This lesson explains why Allah cannot be an attribute subsisting in something else, nor can He be composite or have an equal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on self-subsistence
Read the Arabic text that negates the opposite of being self-subsistent.
Look for the two ways something might not be self-subsistent: being an attribute in a locus or needing a specificator.
2
Understand self-subsistence
Recognize that Allah does not depend on any substrate or external cause for His existence.
This negates any form of accident-hood or contingency.
3
Read the passage on oneness
Read the section that negates the opposite of being one.
Three forms: composition in essence, having a peer, or sharing influence in action.
4
Reflect on Allah's uniqueness
Consider how these negations protect tawḥīd and affirm that Allah alone is the source of all existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِمَّا يَستَحِيلُ في حقهِ تَعالى عِشرونَ صِفةٌ، وَهِي أَضْدَادُ العِشْرِينَ الأُولى. وَهِيَ: العَدَمُ، والحدُوثُ، وَطرُوء العَدَمِ، والمماثَلةُ لِلحَوادِثِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ جِرْماً، أي: تأخُذُ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّةُ قَدْراً مِنَ الفَرَاغ، أوْ يَكُونَ عَرَضاً يَقُومُ بِالجِرْمِ، أو يكُونَ فِي جهةٍ لِلجِرْمِ، أو لهُ هُوَ جِهَةٌ، أو يَتَقَيَّدَ بِمَكَانٍ أو زَمَانٍ، أو تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّة بَالحَوادِثِ، أو يَتَّصِفَ بِالصَّغَرِ، أو الكِبَر، أو يَتَّصفَ بالأغراض في الأفعالِ والأحكام. وَكَذَا يَسْتَجِيلُ عَليهِ تَعَالَى أَنْ لا يَكُونَ قائِماً بِنَفْسِهِ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفةٌ يَقُومُ بِمَحلّ، أَوْ يَحتَاجَ إِلى مُخَصِّص. وكذَا يَسْتَحيلُ عَليهِ تَعالى أَنْ لا يَكونَ واحداً، بِأَنْ يَكونَ مُرَكَّباً فِي ذَاتِهِ، أو يكُونَ لَهُ مُماثل في ذاته، أو في صِفاتِهِ، أو يَكُونَ مَعهُ في الوُجودِ مؤثر في فعل منَ الأَفْعالِ. وكَذا يَستَحيلُ عَليهِ تعالى العَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِنٍ ما، وإيجاد شيء من العالم مَعَ كَراهته لِوُجودِهِ، أي: عَدمِ إرادته لهُ تَعالى، أو مَعَ النُّهولِ، أو الغفلة، أو بالتغليل، أو بالطبع. وكذا يستحيل عليهِ تعالى الجَهْل وَمَا فَي مَعْناهُ بِمَعْلُومٍ مَا. وَالمَوْتُ، وَالصَّممُ، والعَمَى، والبكم. وأضْدَادُ الصِّفاتِ المَعْنويَّةِ واضِحَةٌ مِنْ هذِهِ.
And from what is impossible in His right, exalted, are twenty attributes, and they are the opposites of the first twenty. They are: non-existence (ʿadam), origination (ḥudūth), termination (ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam), similarity to originated things (mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith) by being a body (jirm), meaning that His exalted essence occupies a measure of space, or being an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in a body, or being in a direction (jiha) relative to a body, or having a direction Himself, or being restricted by place or time, or His exalted essence being characterized by originated accidents (ḥawādith), or being characterized by smallness (ṣighar) or bigness (kibar), or being characterized by purposes (aġrāḍ) in actions and judgments. Likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be self-subsistent (qāʾim bi nafsih), such as being an attribute subsisting in a locus, or needing a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ). And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be one (wāḥid), such as being composite (murakkab) in His essence, or having a similar being in essence or attributes, or having with Him in existence an effecter in any action. And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, incapability (ʿajz) of any possible thing, and bringing into existence something from the world while disliking its existence (i.e., not willing it, exalted), or with repugnance (nuhūl), or heedlessness (ghafla), or by compulsion (taghlīl?), or by nature (ṭabʿ). And likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, ignorance (jahl) and what is in its meaning regarding anything known. And death (mawt), deafness (ṣamam), blindness (ʿamā), and muteness (bakam). And the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the twenty impossible attributes for Allah, which are the opposites of the twenty necessary attributes (the six essential + seven of meaning + seven derived). Sanusi lists them with explanations. The first four are: non-existence, origination, termination, and similarity to originated things. He then explains that similarity includes being a body (occupying space), being an accident, having a direction, being restricted by place/time, being subject to accidents, having size (small/big), and having purposes. Then he adds: not being self-subsistent (i.e., needing a locus or specificator), not being one (i.e., composition, having a peer, or sharing influence), incapability, creating while disliking or without will (heedlessness, compulsion, nature), ignorance, and then death, deafness, blindness, muteness. He concludes that the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these (e.g., not being powerful follows from incapability).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Allah can be described as being composed of parts or having a partner in some actions.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly negates composition and partnership in all forms, affirming absolute oneness and independence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is a way that something might not be self-subsistent?
A) Being omnipotent
B) Being an accident subsisting in a body
C) Being eternal
D) Being knowing

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is self-subsistent, needing no one, then why do you sometimes act as if your efforts are the real cause of your success? Will you remind yourself daily that Allah alone is the sustainer?
?
The passage says Allah cannot have a peer in His attributes. Which of His attributes—like mercy, power, or provision—do you tend to attribute to others in your heart? How would your life change if you truly believed He is unique in every quality?

Negating Imperfection: Incapability, Ignorance, and Sensory Deficits
Sanusi enumerates the impossibilities of incapability, ignorance, death, deafness, blindness, and muteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be incapable, ignorant, or subject to death and sensory limitations.

Core Idea
Allah's perfection negates every form of weakness and deficiency.

The final set of impossible attributes covers all forms of imperfection: incapability, ignorance, death, deafness, blindness, and muteness. These negations protect Allah's attributes of power, knowledge, and life. This lesson examines each and shows how they relate to the necessary attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on incapability
Read the Arabic text that negates incapability (ʿajz) and its associated sub-items.
Notice the various ways incapability might manifest, such as creating with dislike, heedlessness, compulsion, or nature.
2
Understand the negation of ignorance
Read the line that negates ignorance (jahl) and everything in its meaning.
This includes all forms of not knowing.
3
Read the negation of death and sensory deficits
Read the lines that negate death, deafness, blindness, and muteness.
These oppose life and sensory perfection.
4
Connect to derived attributes
Notice the concluding remark that the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these.
E.g., incapability negates power, ignorance negates knowledge, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِمَّا يَستَحِيلُ في حقهِ تَعالى عِشرونَ صِفةٌ، وَهِي أَضْدَادُ العِشْرِينَ الأُولى. وَهِيَ: العَدَمُ، والحدُوثُ، وَطرُوء العَدَمِ، والمماثَلةُ لِلحَوادِثِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ جِرْماً، أي: تأخُذُ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّةُ قَدْراً مِنَ الفَرَاغ، أوْ يَكُونَ عَرَضاً يَقُومُ بِالجِرْمِ، أو يكُونَ فِي جهةٍ لِلجِرْمِ، أو لهُ هُوَ جِهَةٌ، أو يَتَقَيَّدَ بِمَكَانٍ أو زَمَانٍ، أو تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ العَليَّة بَالحَوادِثِ، أو يَتَّصِفَ بِالصَّغَرِ، أو الكِبَر، أو يَتَّصفَ بالأغراض في الأفعالِ والأحكام. وَكَذَا يَسْتَجِيلُ عَليهِ تَعَالَى أَنْ لا يَكُونَ قائِماً بِنَفْسِهِ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفةٌ يَقُومُ بِمَحلّ، أَوْ يَحتَاجَ إِلى مُخَصِّص. وكذَا يَسْتَحيلُ عَليهِ تَعالى أَنْ لا يَكونَ واحداً، بِأَنْ يَكونَ مُرَكَّباً فِي ذَاتِهِ، أو يكُونَ لَهُ مُماثل في ذاته، أو في صِفاتِهِ، أو يَكُونَ مَعهُ في الوُجودِ مؤثر في فعل منَ الأَفْعالِ. وكَذا يَستَحيلُ عَليهِ تعالى العَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِنٍ ما، وإيجاد شيء من العالم مَعَ كَراهته لِوُجودِهِ، أي: عَدمِ إرادته لهُ تَعالى، أو مَعَ النُّهولِ، أو الغفلة، أو بالتغليل، أو بالطبع. وكذا يستحيل عليهِ تعالى الجَهْل وَمَا فَي مَعْناهُ بِمَعْلُومٍ مَا. وَالمَوْتُ، وَالصَّممُ، والعَمَى، والبكم. وأضْدَادُ الصِّفاتِ المَعْنويَّةِ واضِحَةٌ مِنْ هذِهِ.
And from what is impossible in His right, exalted, are twenty attributes, and they are the opposites of the first twenty. They are: non-existence (ʿadam), origination (ḥudūth), termination (ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam), similarity to originated things (mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith) by being a body (jirm), meaning that His exalted essence occupies a measure of space, or being an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in a body, or being in a direction (jiha) relative to a body, or having a direction Himself, or being restricted by place or time, or His exalted essence being characterized by originated accidents (ḥawādith), or being characterized by smallness (ṣighar) or bigness (kibar), or being characterized by purposes (aġrāḍ) in actions and judgments. And the opposites of the derived attributes are clear from these.

?

Impossible: Non-Self-Subsistence and Non-Oneness
Sanusi negates the impossibility of non-self-subsistence and non-oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's self-subsistence and oneness are necessary, and why their opposites are impossible.

Core Idea
God cannot depend on anything or be composite.

Sanusi begins by negating two fundamental impossibilities: that God could be dependent on a substrate or need a particularizer, and that He could be composite or have a peer. These establish God's absolute independence and uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces each impossibility with 'وكذا يستحيل'.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The passage contains two distinct impossibilities: non-self-subsistence and non-oneness. Find the conditions that make each impossible.
Non-self-subsistence includes being a quality in a subject or needing a particularizer. Non-oneness includes composition, having a peer, or another independent agent.
3
Reflect on why these are necessary
Consider why God must be self-subsistent and one. The commentary will explain how these negations establish God's independence and uniqueness.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يَسْتَحيلُ عليه تعالى أن لا يكون قائماً بنفسه، بأن يكون صفة يقوم بمحل، أو يحتاج إلى مخصص. وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى أن لا يكون واحداً، بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته، أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته، أو في صفاته، أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال. وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى العجز عن ممكن ما، وإيجاد شيء من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده، أي: عدم إرادته له تعالى، أو مع النهول[؟]، أو الغفلة، أو بالتغليل[؟]، أو بالطبع. وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الجهل وما في معناه بمعلوم ما. والموت، والصمم، والعمى، والبكم. وأضداد الصفات المعنوية واضحة من هذه. وأما الجائز في حقه تعالى ففعل كل ممكن أو تركه.
And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be self-subsistent, by being an attribute subsisting in a subject, or needing a particularizer. And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be one, by being composite in His essence, or that He have a likeness in His essence or in His attributes, or that there be with Him in existence an agent affecting any action. And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, inability regarding any possible thing, and bringing into existence something of the world while He dislikes its existence—i.e., His not willing it—or with [?] drowsiness, or heedlessness, or [?] being unaware, or by nature. And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, ignorance and whatever is in its meaning regarding any known thing. And death, deafness, blindness, and muteness. And the opposites of the intrinsic attributes are clear from these. As for the possible with respect to Him, exalted, it is doing every possible thing or leaving it.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the impossible (mustaḥīl) attributes of God and a statement of the possible (jāʾiz) attribute. It begins by listing what cannot be attributed to God. First, it negates that God could fail to be self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-nafsihi): He cannot be a quality subsisting in a substrate, nor can He need a particularizer. Second, it negates that He could fail to be one (wāḥid): His essence cannot be composite, He cannot have a peer in essence or attributes, and no other agent can act independently alongside Him. Third, it negates inability (ʿajz) regarding any possible thing, and negates that He could bring about something while disliking it—i.e., without willing it—or while in a state of drowsiness, heedlessness, unawareness, or by nature—all of which are deficiencies. Fourth, it negates ignorance (jahl) of any known thing. Fifth, it negates literal death, deafness, blindness, and muteness. Then it notes that the opposites of the intrinsic attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya) are obvious from these negations. Finally, it states the single possible attribute: God may do or leave any contingent act—there is no necessity or impossibility for Him regarding created actions.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
وحدانية
مركب
مماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God is composite like created beings or has a partner in divinity.
Correction: God's oneness negates any composition or peer, and His self-subsistence negates any dependency.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is impossible for God according to this passage?
A) Being self-subsistent
B) Being composite in essence
C) Having power over all possibles
D) Being one

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence and oneness are necessary attributes whose opposites are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not self-subsistent, He would need something else to exist. How does this realization affect your reliance on created things? Do you ever act as if something other than God is necessary for your existence? Commit this week to consciously acknowledge your absolute dependence on Him alone.
?
God's oneness negates any likeness in His essence or attributes. Are there any attributes you ascribe to God that resemble created things, even subtly? How can you purify your conception of Him? Identify one attribute and reframe it according to divine transcendence.
?
The passage says it is impossible for any other agent to affect any action alongside God. In what areas of your life are you tempted to believe that your own effort or someone else's is the ultimate cause of outcomes, rather than God alone? What practical step can you take today to attribute all results to Him?

Impossible: Incapacity and Creation Against Will
Sanusi negates incapacity and creation against God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God cannot be incapable of any possible thing nor create something without will.

Core Idea
God's power is absolute and His will is always effective.

After establishing God's essential independence and oneness, Sanusi now negates any deficiency in God's power and will. God cannot be incapable of any possible thing, nor can He bring something into existence while disliking it or through inattention.

Impossible: Ignorance, Death, Sensory Deficiencies, and the Possible Attribute
Sanusi negates ignorance, death, deafness, blindness, muteness, and states the possible attribute.

God is all-knowing, ever-living, and free in His actions.

First, it negates that God could fail to be self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-nafsihi): He cannot be a quality subsisting in a substrate, nor can He need a particularizer. Second, it negates that He could fail to be one (wāḥid): His essence cannot be composite, He cannot have a peer in essence or attributes, and no other agent can act independently alongside Him. Third, it negates inability (ʿajz) regarding any possible thing, and negates that He could bring about something while disliking it—i.e., without willing it—or while in a state of drowsiness, heedlessness, unawareness, or by nature—all of which are deficiencies. Fourth, it negates ignorance (jahl) of any known thing. Fifth, it negates literal death, deafness, blindness, and muteness. Then it notes that the opposites of the intrinsic attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya) are obvious from these negations. Finally, it states the single possible attribute: God may do or leave any contingent act—there is no necessity or impossibility for Him regarding created actions.

Key Terms
جهل
موت
صمم
عمى
بكم
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God could be ignorant of future events or that He must create the world.
Correction: God knows all things and is free to create or not create.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is impossible for God according to this passage?
A) Having knowledge of all things
B) Being alive
C) Being subject to death
D) Doing any possible act

Key Takeaway
God is all-knowing, ever-living, free from sensory defects, and has the free choice to act or abstain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God knows all things and is free from ignorance. But when you face a situation you don't understand, do you truly trust that God's knowledge is sufficient, or do you insist on having all the answers yourself? Commit to prayerfully surrender one unanswered question to Him today.
?
God is ever-living, all-hearing, all-seeing, and free from death and sensory deficiency. How does this reality change the way you live your daily life knowing that every word you speak and every action you take is witnessed by a perfect, living God? What will you do differently tomorrow because of this truth?
?
The passage says God has the freedom to do or leave every possible thing. Is there an area of your life where you are trying to dictate God's actions—expecting Him to act in a certain way—rather than submitting to His free choice? Repent of any demand you have placed on God and ask for a heart that embraces His wisdom, whatever He chooses.

Proof of God's Necessary Everlastingness
Sanusi proves that God must be everlasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be everlasting based on the logical necessity of His existence.

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He would not be necessary—and the necessary cannot possibly fail to exist.

Sanusi presents a concise proof that God is not only pre-eternal but also everlasting. Using the same logic that established His necessity, he shows that the possibility of non-existence would contradict His essential nature. This argument reinforces that God’s existence is absolute and without end.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument from a conditional statement to a necessary conclusion.
The proof relies on the distinction between necessary and possible existence.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: premise (if non-existence possible), consequence (negation of pre-eternity), reason (existence becomes possible), further implication (possible implies contingent), and appeal to prior proof.
3
Connect to previous lessons
Recall that God's necessity and pre-eternity were already established. This proof builds on that foundation to show that everlastingness is also necessary.
This shows the interconnectedness of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب البقاء له تعالى: فلأنه لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدم لانتفى عنه القدم، لكون وجوده حينئذ يصير جائزاً لا واجباً، والجائز لا يكون وجوده إلا حادثاً، كيف وقد سبق قريباً وجوب قدمه تعالى وبقائه.
As for the proof of the necessity of everlastingness (baqāʾ) for Him, exalted: it is because if non-existence could possibly affect Him, pre-eternity would be negated from Him, because then His existence would become possible (jāʾiz) rather than necessary (wājib), and the possible does not exist except as contingent, especially since the necessity of His pre-eternity and everlastingness has already been established.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage proves that God must be everlasting (bāqī), i.e., He cannot cease to exist. The argument is: if it were possible for God to become non-existent, then His existence would not be necessary (wājib) but merely possible (jāʾiz). But whatever is possible in itself must come into existence from non-existence, i.e., be contingent. This would contradict His already proven necessity and pre-eternity. So everlastingness is a necessary attribute.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدم
وجوب
جواز
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's everlastingness means He will simply endure forever in time, like an ongoing timeline.
Correction: In this context, everlastingness is a logical consequence of God's necessary existence: since He cannot not exist, He has no end. It is not about duration but about impossibility of non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that God must be everlasting?
A) Because scripture says so
B) Because if He could perish, His existence would be possible, not necessary, contradicting His necessity
C) Because He is powerful
D) Because He is self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
God's everlastingness is necessary because if He could cease to exist, His existence would be contingent, contradicting His proven necessity and pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence could possibly cease, what would that imply about your own existence and purpose? How does the certainty of God's everlastingness shape your daily actions and decisions?
?
Sanusi proves that if non-existence could affect God, He would be contingent. Do you sometimes act as if God's existence or attributes depend on your circumstances? How can you affirm His absolute necessity in your heart today?
?
Knowing that God's everlastingness is as necessary as His existence, how does that affect your view of your own mortality? What would change if you truly internalized that God is the only necessary being?

Proof of God's Self-Subsistence
Sanusi proves God's self-subsistence with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God is self-subsistent through two logical arguments, and why self-subsistence is necessary for God.

Core Idea
God cannot depend on anything else for His existence.

Sanusi now turns to proving that God is self-subsistent (qiyām bi-nafsihi), meaning He does not depend on a substrate or a cause for His existence. He gives two concise arguments: one from the impossibility of God being an attribute, and another from the impossibility of Him being contingent. This passage is a model of terse kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if He needed... He would be...'
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two conditional arguments. Find where each begins and ends.
The first argument starts with 'لو احتاج تعالى إلى محل' and the second with 'ولو احتاج إلى مخصص'.
3
Work through the first argument
Follow the logic: if God needed a substrate, He would be an attribute. But attributes cannot have intrinsic and consequential attributes, while God must have them. Therefore God is not an attribute and so does not need a substrate.
This proves self-subsistence via negating attribute-hood.
4
Work through the second argument
If God needed a particularizer, He would be contingent. But contingency contradicts His proven pre-eternity and everlastingness.
This proves self-subsistence via negating contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب قيامه تعالى بنفسه: فلأنه لو احتاج تعالى إلى محل لكان صفة، والصفة لا تتصف بصفات المعاني ولا المعنوية، ومولانا جل وعز يجب اتصافه بهما، فليس بصفة، ولو احتاج إلى مخصص لكان حادثاً، كيف وقد قام البرهان على وجوب قدمه تعالى وبقائه.
As for the proof of the necessity of His being self-subsistent (qiyām bi-nafsihi), exalted: it is because if He, exalted, needed a substrate, He would be an attribute; and an attribute is not described by the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) nor by the intrinsic-consequential attributes (al-maʿnawiyya), while our Lord, majestic and sublime, must be described by both; so He is not an attribute. And if He needed a particularizer, He would be contingent, especially since the proof has been established for the necessity of His pre-eternity and everlastingness.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage proves God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi-nafsihi) using two arguments. First, if God needed a substrate (maḥall) in which to subsist, He would be an attribute (ṣifa) existing in another. But attributes cannot themselves be described by the essential attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿānī: power, will, knowledge, life) nor by the intrinsic-consequential attributes (al-maʿnawiyya: being powerful, willing, knowing, living). Since God must be described by both categories, He cannot be an attribute; hence, He is self-subsistent. Second, if God needed a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring Him into existence, He would be contingent (ḥādith). But contingency contradicts His proven pre-eternity and everlastingness. Thus, God is necessarily self-subsistent.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
صفة
محل
صفات المعاني
صفات معنوية
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence means God is independent of all relations (including relations to creation).
Correction: Self-subsistence means God does not depend on a substrate or cause for His existence, but He still has relations to creation such as knowledge of them.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that God cannot be an attribute?
A) Because attributes are contingent
B) Because attributes cannot have intrinsic attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) and intrinsic-consequential attributes
C) Because attributes are multiple
D) Because attributes need a subject

Key Takeaway
God is necessarily self-subsistent (qiyām bi-nafsihi), proven by the impossibility of Him being an attribute or contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely self-subsistent—needing nothing from you—why do you sometimes act as if your worship is for His benefit rather than your own? How can you purify your intention today to acknowledge your absolute need for Him?
?
The proof shows that God does not subsist in any created thing. Does your heart rely on any person, status, or possession as if it were self-sufficient? What would it look like to truly depend only on God for your existence and well-being?
?
God's self-subsistence means you are entirely dependent on Him at every moment. When you feel self-sufficient in your daily life, what concrete reminder can you place in your routine to recall that your very existence is a gift from the Self-Subsistent One?

Proof of God's Essential Attributes: Power, Will, Knowledge, Life
Sanusi proves four essential attributes via a single conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of contingent things proves that God must have power, will, knowledge, and life.

Core Idea
Contingent things exist, so their Creator must have the attributes to create them.

Sanusi now presents a concise but powerful proof for four essential attributes of God. He argues from the observed existence of contingent things back to the necessary attributes of their Creator. This lesson unpacks that single logical step and shows why these four attributes are inseparable from the act of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi states the proof in one conditional statement.
The proof is very compact; every word matters.
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof is a conditional: 'If any of these attributes were absent, then no contingent thing would exist.' Since contingent things do exist, the antecedent must be false—i.e., the attributes are present.
This is a standard argument from effect to cause.
3
Connect each attribute to creation
Reflect on why each attribute is necessary: power to bring about existence, will to choose which things to create, knowledge to know what is created, and life as a precondition for will and knowledge.
Life is included because inanimate beings cannot will or know.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
Some might think these attributes are separate parts of God or that He needs them to be perfect. Sanusi's argument shows they are necessary for the very existence of the universe.
They are real but not separate from the essence in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب اتصافه تعالى بالقدرة والإرادة والعلم والحياة: فلأنه لو انتفى شيء منها لما وجد شيء من الحوادث.
As for the proof of the necessity of His being described with power (qudra), will (irāda), knowledge (ʿilm), and life (ḥayāh), exalted: it is because if any of them were negated, nothing of contingent things would exist.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
Sanusi gives a collective proof for four essential attributes: power, will, knowledge, and life. The argument is that contingent things (al-ḥawādith) obviously exist because we see them. Their existence requires a creator who is powerful (able to bring them about), willing (choosing to create them), knowing (what He creates), and alive (since life is a prerequisite for knowledge and will). If any of these attributes were missing, the contingent things could not come into existence. Therefore, God must possess all four. This is a standard kalam argument from the existence of contingent effects to the necessary attributes of the cause.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
علم
حياة
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these attributes are unnecessary or that God could create without them.
Correction: The proof shows that without any one of the four, creation would be impossible. They are necessary conditions for the existence of contingent things.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's proof, what would happen if God did not have power, will, knowledge, and life?
A) He would still be able to create
B) Nothing of contingent things would exist
C) He would be imperfect but could still create
D) Creation would be limited but still possible

Key Takeaway
The existence of the universe itself proves that God has power, will, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of your existence depends directly on God's power, will, knowledge, and life, how would you start your day differently tomorrow? What specific change would you commit to?
?
When you face a situation that feels beyond your control, does your heart turn to the reality that God's power is the only power that truly creates outcomes? How can you train yourself to live in that truth today?
?
Consider the attribute of God's knowledge: He knows every detail of your life. How does that awareness shape the way you speak and act when no one else is watching? Write one concrete action you will take this week to honor that knowledge.

Proof of God's Hearing, Seeing, and Speech
Sanusi proves hearing, seeing, and speech from scripture and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational arguments for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and why their opposites are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God must have hearing, sight, and speech because their absence would imply deficiency.

Sanusi now turns to the attributes of hearing (samʿ), sight (baṣar), and speech (kalām). He presents a concise proof combining transmitted evidence from the Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus, with a rational argument that if God lacked these attributes, He would be subject to their deficient opposites. This passage illustrates how kalam integrates revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then read the literal translation. Notice the two parts: first the transmitted proof, then the rational proof.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Sanusi gives two proofs: scriptural (Book, Sunna, consensus) and rational (if not these attributes then their opposites would be necessary, which is impossible). Understand each.
The rational proof uses the principle that if a perfection attribute is denied, its opposite imperfection must be affirmed.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why can't God have deficiencies? Because deficiency implies need, and God is self-sufficient. This lesson establishes that these attributes are necessary for God.
This connects to the broader principle that God must possess all perfection attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما برهان وجوب السمع له تعالى والبصر والكلام فالكتاب والسنة والإجماع. وأيضاً لو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها، وهي نقائص، والنقص عليه تعالى محال.
As for the proof of the necessity of hearing (samʿ) for Him, exalted, and sight (baṣar) and speech (kalām), it is the Book, the Sunna, and consensus. And also, if He were not described with them, it would follow that He be described with their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency upon Him, exalted, is impossible.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
Sanusi proves that God necessarily has hearing, sight, and speech. He gives two types of evidence. First, the transmitted evidence: the Qur'an (al-Kitāb), the prophetic tradition (al-Sunna), and the consensus of the Muslim community (al-Ijmāʿ) affirm these attributes. Second, a rational argument: if God lacked these attributes, He would have to have their opposites—deafness, blindness, muteness—which are deficiencies (naqāʾiṣ). Since deficiency is impossible for God, He must possess hearing, sight, and speech. This illustrates that for each attribute, its opposite is a perfection deficiency; God must be free from all imperfection.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
كلام
كتاب
سنة
إجماع
نقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's hearing and seeing are like human hearing and seeing, requiring physical organs like ears and eyes.
Correction: God's attributes of hearing and seeing are eternal and without physical organs. They are appropriate to His majesty and are not dependent on creation.

Checkpoint
What rational reason does Sanusi give for God having hearing, sight, and speech?
A) Because they are mentioned in the Qur'an
B) Because without them God would have their opposites, which are deficiencies
C) Because God created ears and eyes
D) Because the prophets asked for them

Key Takeaway
God necessarily possesses hearing, sight, and speech because their absence would imply deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel alone and that no one hears or sees you, do you truly believe that God hears and sees you at every moment? How would your daily actions change if you lived with this certainty, beginning now?
?
The proof says that lacking hearing, sight, and speech would be a deficiency. But in your own life, where do you tolerate spiritual deficiencies—like neglecting prayer, ignoring God's commands, or failing to seek His forgiveness? Will you commit today to addressing one specific deficiency you have been avoiding?
?
God's perfection demands that you strive to reflect His attributes within your capacity. Are you consciously cultivating listening to others, seeing their needs, and speaking truth and kindness? What is one practical step you can take today to mirror these divine qualities in your interactions?
