Proof for God's Attributes: Power, Will, Knowledge, and Life
Sanusi gives a concise proof for four divine attributes based on the existence of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God must possess the attributes of power, will, knowledge, and life, and why the existence of events depends on them.

Core Idea
Without divine attributes, no event could ever occur.

Sanusi now presents a concise proof for four essential divine attributes. He argues that the mere existence of originated events in the world forces us to affirm that God has power, will, knowledge, and life. This short passage packs a profound theological argument that is foundational in Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the proof in a single conditional sentence: if any attribute were missing, no events would exist.
The passage is very concise—every word carries weight.
2
Understand the logical necessity
Think about why each attribute is necessary. Events require an agent with power to cause them, will to choose them, knowledge to direct them, and life to be capable of action. If any were absent, events could not occur.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the opposite.
3
Reflect on why life is included
Life is included because only a living being can have power, will, and knowledge. In Ash'ari theology, life is the foundation for the other attributes.
This connection shows the coherence of the divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجوبِ اتِّصَافِهِ تعالى بالقُدْرَةِ والإرادة والعِلمِ والحَياةِ: فلأنَّهُ لو انتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنها لما وُجِدَ شيءٌ مِنَ الحَوَادِثِ.
As for the proof of the necessity of His being described with power, will, knowledge, and life: because if any of them were absent, nothing of the originated things would exist.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage proves that God must possess the attributes of power (qudra), will (irada), knowledge ('ilm), and life (haya). The argument is straightforward: the world contains originated events (hawadith). For these events to exist, there must be an agent who has the power to bring them about, the will to choose them, the knowledge of them, and the life to be capable of action. If any of these attributes were missing, no event could come into existence.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
علم
حياة
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's attributes are separate entities or that they are not necessary.
Correction: Sanusi shows that these attributes are necessarily true of God because without them, no events could exist. They are not separate from His essence but are eternal attributes subsisting in it.

Checkpoint
Why must God have power, will, knowledge, and life?
A) Because without them, God would be imperfect.
B) Because otherwise no originated thing would exist.
C) Because they are mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because humans have them.

Key Takeaway
The existence of any event in the universe proves that God has power, will, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one event in your life today that felt random or unimportant. Could it have happened without God's power, will, knowledge, and life? How will you remind yourself before your next action that every detail depends on these attributes?
?

?
You exist right now because God is alive. Does your daily routine reflect that you are sustained by a living God, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What can you change tomorrow to live in conscious dependence on His life?

Proof for God's Attributes of Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi proves God has hearing, sight, and speech through scripture and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sanusi proves God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech through both scriptural and rational arguments, based on the impossibility of divine deficiency.

Core Idea
God's hearing, sight, and speech are necessary to avoid imperfection.

Sanusi now addresses three more divine attributes: hearing, sight, and speech. He offers a concise proof that combines revealed evidence — the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus — with a rational argument: lacking these would mean having their opposites, which are defects. Defects cannot be attributed to Allah, so these attributes are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the two distinct parts of the proof.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the logical structure.
2
Identify the two lines of proof
Sanusi gives two types of evidence: scriptural (Book, Sunnah, consensus) and rational (if He lacked them, He would have defects). Find each in the text.
The rational argument is especially important in kalam because it does not rely on revelation alone.
3
Reflect on the rational argument
Consider why the opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness) are considered deficiencies. This shows that kalam reasoning starts from God's perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بُرهانُ وُجُوبِ السَّمْعِ لهُ تعالى وَالبَصرِ وَالكلام فالكتاب والسنة والإجماعُ. وأيضاً لَو لَمْ يَتَّصِفُ بِها لزِمَ أَنْ يَتَّصِفَ بِأَضدادِهَا، وَهِيَ نقائص، والنقصُ عَلَيْهِ تَعالى مُحالٌ.
As for the proof of the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech for Him, Most High: it is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. Also, if He were not described with them, it would necessitate that He be described with their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency upon Him, Most High, is impossible.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage proves that God must possess the attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and speech (kalam). The proof has two parts: First, scriptural evidence—the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus (ijma') affirm these attributes for God. Second, a rational argument: if God did not possess these attributes, He would have to possess their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects. Defects are impossible for God, so He must possess hearing, sight, and speech.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
كلام
كتاب
سنة
إجماع
نقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are physical like human senses.
Correction: In kalam, these are eternal attributes that are not comparable to human senses. They are perfect and beyond physical limitations.

Checkpoint
What rational argument does Sanusi give for God's hearing, sight, and speech?
A) They are necessary for creation.
B) Their opposites are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God.
C) Humans have them.
D) They are mentioned in the Quran only.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that God must possess hearing, sight, and speech because scripture commands belief in them and because their opposites would be deficiencies, which cannot apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God's speech is eternal and perfect. When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as the very speech of God or as a human book? What commitment can you make today to honour His speech?

The Permissibility of God's Actions
Sanusi proves God's actions on possibles are permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that God's actions concerning possible things are permissible (ja'iz), neither necessary nor impossible.

Core Idea
Possible things remain possible only by God's free choice, not by compulsion.

Sanusi now turns to what God must do—or not do—regarding created things. He argues that reason alone cannot force God to act in any particular way toward the world of possibles. The following passage presents a crisp logical demonstration that God's actions are purely permissible, preserving both His freedom and the nature of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the logical structure: 'if... then...' and the conclusion 'not intelligible.'
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three rational categories
The passage mentions necessary (wajib), impossible (mustahil), and permissible (ja'iz). Understand how each relates to God's actions.
3
Trace the argument's logic
Sanusi argues: If any possible action were necessary or impossible for God, that possible would change its nature. But that is not rationally conceivable. Therefore, God's actions are merely permissible.
This is a proof by contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it defends God's absolute freedom and the coherence of the category 'possible' in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بُرْهانُ كوْنِ فِعل المُمْكِناتِ أو تركها جَائِزاً في حَقِّهِ تَعالى: فلأنَّهُ لَوْ وَجَبَ عليهِ تَعالى شَيْءٌ مِنها عَقلاً، أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب المُمْكِنُ واجِباً أو مستحيلاً، وذلك لا يُعْقَلُ.
As for the proof that doing or leaving possible things is permissible (ja'iz) in His right, Most High: because if something from them were necessary for Him, Most High, by reason, or impossible by reason, the possible would turn into necessary or impossible, and that is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage proves that God's actions regarding possible things (mumkinat) are neither necessary (wajib) nor impossible (mustahil) but permissible (ja'iz). The argument: If any possible action were rationally necessary for God, or rationally impossible, then that possible thing would change its nature to necessary or impossible. But possible things are defined as being able to exist or not exist. Changing their nature is not rationally conceivable. Therefore, God's actions toward possibles are merely permissible, meaning He may do them or leave them without constraint.

Key Terms
فعل الممكنات
جائز
واجب
مستحيل
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God must act in a certain way toward creation (e.g., always create the best).
Correction: Sanusi argues that reason alone does not make any possible action necessary or impossible for God; His actions are free and permissible.

Checkpoint
Why must God's actions toward possible things be permissible (ja'iz)?
A) Because God is powerful.
B) Because if they were necessary or impossible, the possible would change its nature.
C) Because reason cannot decide.
D) Because God is just.

Key Takeaway
God's actions regarding possible things are permissible, preserving both His freedom and the rational coherence of the category 'possible.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever think of God as obligated to reward you for your good deeds? After reading this passage, consider: If God's actions are merely permissible, is your sense of entitlement to His reward a form of imposing necessity on Him? How can you let go of that expectation and worship Him freely?
?
How does the truth that God is not compelled by reason affect your trust in His decisions, especially when you face trials you cannot understand? Are you willing to submit to a God whose actions are not bound by your logic? What would change in your heart if you fully embraced this?
?
If God's actions are purely voluntary, what does that mean for your prayers? Do you approach Him as one who is free to respond or refuse, or do you secretly assume He must answer? Can you pray with genuine humility, knowing He is not obliged to listen? How might that deepen your reliance on His mercy?

Divine Attributes

Attributes Derived from Allah's Self-Sufficiency
Sanusi enumerates attributes derived from istighna and derives further negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of Allah's absolute self-sufficiency (istighna) logically entails His essential attributes, negates any purpose in His actions, and precludes any obligation upon Him.

Core Idea
Allah's self-sufficiency is the key that unlocks His attributes and transcends all need.

In this passage, Sanusi applies the first pillar of uluhiyya—Allah's absolute independence from all else—to derive a set of essential attributes. He also draws important conclusions: that Allah has no purposes behind His actions, and that nothing can be morally obligatory upon Him. Understanding this logical derivation is central to the Islamic conception of God's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text slowly, then refer to the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi begins with 'As for His self-sufficiency...' and then lists attributes.
The Arabic uses 'yūjibu' (necessitates) which is a key logical term.
2
Identify the six attributes derived from istighna
List the six attributes: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to created things, self-subsistence, and freedom from deficiencies. Understand why each is necessary given self-sufficiency.
These are the six essential attributes common in Ash'ari theology.
3
Examine the inclusion of hearing, sight, and speech
Sanusi says these are included in the above. See the reasoning: if they were not necessary, Allah would need an originator or locus, contradicting self-sufficiency.
4
Understand the negation of purposes
Read the part about 'tanzīhuhu 'an al-a‘rāḍ' – Allah is free from purposes. If He had purposes, He would need something to achieve them, which is impossible.
5
Grasp the conclusion: no obligation upon Allah
Finally, Sanusi argues that no act of contingent things is obligatory upon Allah, because that would imply need for perfection. This is a key Ash'ari principle.
This guards against the Mu'tazilite idea that God must do the optimal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا استغناؤُهُ جلّ وعَلاَ عَنْ كلّ ما سواه فهوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تعالى الوُجودَ والقدمَ والبَقاء والمُخَالَفَةَ للحَوَادِثِ والقِيامَ بِالنَّفس والتَنزُّه عن النقائص. ويدخل في ذلك: وُجوبُ الشمع لَهُ تعالى والبَصرِ وَالكلام؛ إذ لو لَمْ تَجِبْ لَهُ تَعالى هذِهِ الصَّفاتُ لكان مُحتاجاً إلى المُحْدِثِ، أو المحلّ، أو مَنْ يَدفعُ عَنهُ النَّقائِصَ. وَيؤخذُ مِنْهُ: تنزُّهُهُ تَعالى عَنِ الأعْراضِ في أفعاله وأحكامه، وإلا لزِمَ افتقارُهُ إِلى مَا يُحصِل غَرَضَهُ، كيفَ وهُوَ جلّ وعَلا الغَنِيُّ عَنْ كُلَّ مَا سواه؟ ويُؤخَذُ مِنْهُ أَيْضاً أَنَّهُ لا يجِبُ عَلَيْهِ تَعالى فِعْلُ شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُمْكِناتِ وَلا تركه؛ إذ لَوْ وَجَبَ عَلَيْهِ تعالى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا عَقْلاً كالنَّوابِ مَثَلاً لكانَ جَلْ وعزّ مُفتقراً إلى ذلك الشَّيء لِيَتَكَمَّل بِهِ؛ إذْ لا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّهِ جَلّ وَعَزّ إِلا ما هُوَ كمال له، كيف وهُوَ الغَني جلّ وَعَلا عَنْ كُلِّ مَا سَواهُ؟
As for His self-sufficiency, may He be glorified and exalted, from everything other than Him, it necessitates for Him, exalted, existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to created things, self-subsistence, and freedom from deficiencies. And included in that is the obligatoriness of hearing (al-sam‘) for Him, exalted, and sight (al-basar) and speech (al-kalam); for if these attributes were not necessary for Him, He would be in need of an originator, or a locus, or someone to repel deficiencies from Him. And taken from it is His transcendence, exalted, from purposes (a‘rad) in His actions and rulings; otherwise, His need would follow to that which achieves His purpose, how can that be when He, glorified and exalted, is the Self-Sufficient from everything other than Him? And taken from it also is that nothing of contingent things (al-mumkinat) is obligatory upon Him, exalted, to do or to leave; for if something of them were obligatory upon Him rationally, such as rewards for example, then He, glorified and mighty, would be dependent on that thing to be perfected by it; for nothing is obligatory in His right, glorified and mighty, except what is a perfection for Him, how can that be when He is the Self-Sufficient, glorified and exalted, from everything other than Him?
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage derives the essential divine attributes from the first pillar of uluhiyya, istighna’ (self-sufficiency). Sanusi explains that Allah's absolute independence logically necessitates six attributes: existence (wujūd), pre-eternity (qidam), everlastingness (baqā’), dissimilarity to created things (mukhālafah li-l-ḥawādith), self-subsistence (qiyām bi-l-nafs), and transcendence above imperfections (tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ). Then he adds that included in these is the necessity of the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, because if these were not necessary, Allah would be dependent on an external cause or a locus, contradicting His self-sufficiency. He further derives that Allah is free from any purpose or aim (gharaḍ) in His actions, since having a purpose would imply need. Finally, he concludes that no act is morally obligatory upon Allah, because obligation would imply that He needs something to perfect Himself, which contradicts His absolute wealth. This passage establishes key points in Sunni theology: that Allah has essential attributes and that He is above any compulsion or need.

بقاء
مخالفة للحوادث
قيام بالنفس
تنزه عن النقائص
سمع
بصر
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Allah is obligated to reward the righteous or punish the wicked as a matter of justice.
Correction: Sanusi argues that no act is obligatory upon Allah because obligation would imply need. Allah's actions are not subject to external obligations.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does Allah's self-sufficiency (istighna) imply about the possibility of any act being obligatory upon Him?
A) It implies that some acts, like rewarding the righteous, are obligatory
B) It implies that nothing is obligatory upon Him because obligation would entail need
C) It implies that only acts of perfecting are obligatory
D) It implies that all acts are permissible but some are necessary

Key Takeaway
Allah's self-sufficiency logically entails all necessary attributes and negates any need, purpose, or obligation in His being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or perform good deeds, do you ever catch yourself feeling that Allah needs your worship? How does the truth that He is absolutely self-sufficient transform the very nature of your devotion? Will you commit to worshiping Him purely out of love and gratitude, not out of a sense of filling a need in Him?
?
Think of a time you were frustrated because your dua was not answered as you wished. Did you assume that Allah was obligated to give you what you asked? How does this lesson's teaching—that no act is obligatory upon Allah—reshape your expectation and patience? Can you release the feeling of entitlement and submit fully to His wisdom?
?
This passage reveals that Allah has no purpose (gharaḍ) in His actions—He does not act to achieve anything for Himself. How does this confront your tendency to attribute human motivations to Allah? When you struggle to understand His decrees, can you trust that His actions are free from need, even when they seem 'pointless' from your perspective? Write one practical step to train your heart to embrace this transcendence.

Divine Attributes

Deriving Four Attributes from Dependence
Sanusi derives the divine attributes of life, power, will, and knowledge from the fact that all else depends on Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi logically derives four essential divine attributes (life, power, will, knowledge) from the fact that all creation depends on Allah.

Core Idea
Dependence of creation demands that Allah be living, powerful, willing, and knowing.

Sanusi now turns to the second fundamental observation about Allah's relationship to creation: absolute dependence. From this single fact, he extracts four necessary attributes. The argument is tight: if even one of these attributes were missing, nothing contingent could exist, which would contradict the universal dependence we observe. Let's examine the logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the cause-and-effect structure: 'wa ammā ... fa-huwa yūjibu ...'. This is a concise logical proof.
The phrase 'kayfa wa huwa alladhī yaftaqiru ilayhi kullu mā siwāhu' is a rhetorical question reinforcing the conclusion.
2
Identify the four attributes and the logical connection
The passage directly states that dependence (iftiqār) necessitates four attributes: life (al-ḥayāh), universal power (ʿumūm al-qudrah), will (al-irādah), and knowledge (al-ʿilm). Try to see each as a necessary condition for creating and sustaining contingent beings.
3
Understand the hypothetical proof (condition contrary to fact)
Sanusi provides a conditional proof: if any of these attributes were absent, it would be impossible for any contingent thing (ḥawādith) to exist. Since contingent things do exist, the attributes must be present. This is a foundational argument in kalām for divine attributes.
This argument assumes that creation's dependence is evident. Sanusi is building on the earlier observation that everything other than Allah is contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا افتقارُ كُلِّ مَا سِواهُ إِلَيْهِ جَلَّ وَعَزَّ فَهُوَ يُوجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى الحَياةَ وَعمُومَ القُدْرَةِ والإِرَادَة والعِلمِ؛ إذْ لَوِ انتفى شَيْءٌ مِنها لَما أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يُوجَدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الحَوادِثِ، فَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ، كَيْفَ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَيْهِ كُلُّ مَا سِوَاهُ؟
And as for the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him, may He be glorified and mighty, it necessitates for Him, exalted, life (al-ḥayāh), and the universality of power (al-qudrah), and will (al-irādah), and knowledge (al-ʿilm); for if any of these were absent, it would not be possible for anything from contingent things to exist, so nothing would depend upon Him — how can that be when it is He upon whom everything other than Him depends?
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage derives four essential attributes from the second pillar of uluhiyya, iftiqār (dependence). Sanusi argues that since everything other than Allah depends entirely on Him for existence, Allah must possess life, absolute power, will, and knowledge. The reasoning is that without these attributes, it would be impossible for any contingent being (ḥādith) to come into existence; if existence were possible without these attributes, then things would not need Allah, contradicting the fact that all things depend on Him. This is a concise logical proof for the necessity of these four attributes.

Key Terms
افتقار
حياة
قدرة
إرادة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply stating that creation depends on Allah is enough to affirm His attributes without need for proof.
Correction: Sanusi provides a logical proof: if any attribute were missing, contingent existence would be impossible, which would contradict the very dependence we observe. Thus, the attributes are rationally necessitated, not assumed.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's argument, what would happen if Allah lacked any one of the four attributes (life, power, will, knowledge)?
A) Something could still exist by itself.
B) Nothing contingent could exist.
C) Dependence would still be universal.
D) Only some things would depend on Him.

Key Takeaway
Allah's life, power, will, and knowledge are logically necessitated by the fact that everything other than Him depends entirely on Him for existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Everything depends on Allah for every moment of existence, including your next heartbeat. Why do you still act as if your success, health, or provisions are in your own hands? Name one decision you made today that ignored this dependence.
?
You say Allah is Knowing, Willing, and Powerful, yet you spend hours worrying about a future He already controls. What specific fear are you holding onto that contradicts His attributes? Will you genuinely surrender it to Him today by an act of trust?
?
If Allah lacked any of these attributes, nothing could exist—including you reading this. How does that truth reframe your sense of entitlement? What is one thing you often take for granted that you can now recognize as a direct gift from the Living, Powerful, Willing, Knowing Creator?

The Meaning of 'Name' in Divine Context
Definition of 'name' and the principle of derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'name' (ism) applies to both derivative and non-derivative divine names, and that derived names indicate attributes.

Core Idea
Every derived divine name points to a specific attribute of Allah.

Before examining the specific names, the author clarifies what 'name' means when applied to Allah. This is not a mere linguistic point: it determines how we derive knowledge of Allah's attributes from His names. The passage explains that 'name' includes both names that indicate the Essence alone, like 'Allah', and those that indicate an attribute, like 'al-Knowing'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of names
Read the passage and note the distinction between names that indicate the Essence alone (like 'Allah') and those that indicate the Essence with an attribute (like 'al-Knowing').
The author also mentions names that are allowed by shar'i ruling (hukman) like 'as-Sani'.
2
Understand the principle of derivation
The passage states that derived names (al-mushtaqqat) have their origin established for the one named. This means that if Allah is called 'al-Qadir', then He must possess the attribute of power.
This is a key theological principle.
3
See the role of consensus and school
Notice that the author refers to consensus (ijma') for names like 'al-Mawjud', and to a particular madhhab for 'al-Mutakallim'. This shows the scope of acceptable names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقَرَّرَ بعضُ مشايخنا الإشارة إلى العقائد بوجةٍ آخَرَ وهو أن الاسم عام في المشتق وغيره؛ لأن المراد به ما دَلَّ على الذات بمجردها كـ (الله)، أو باعتبار الصفة كالعالم، سواءٌ وَرَدَ الإذن به حقيقةً كما ذَكَره، أو حُكْماً كالصانع والموجود والواجب، فإن الثلاثة ثابتة بالإجماع، وكمُتَكَلَّم بناءً على مذهب من يكتفي بورود المادة، والمشتقاتُ ثَبَتَ مبدأ اشتقاقها لمن سُمّي بها، فموجود يدلُّ على الوجود، وقديم يدلُّ على القِدَمِ، وباقٍ يدلُّ على البقاء، وقُدُّوسٌ يدلُّ على المخالَفَةِ للحوادث، وغني يدلُّ على القيام بالنفس، وواحد يدلُّ على الوحدانية، وقادر يدلُّ على القدرة، ومُرِيدٌ يدلُّ على الإرادة، وعالِمٌ يدلُّ على العِلْمِ، وحي يدلُّ على الحياة، وسميع يدلُّ على السمع وبصير يدلُّ على البصر ومتكلّم يدلُّ على الكلام. والمَعْنَوِيَّة عند القائلين بها واضِحَةٌ من المعاني والمستحيلات مفهومةً من ثبوت الصفات المذكورة، والجائزات مفهومةٌ من نحو قادِرٍ ومُرِيدِ ، والرحمن الرحيم: المُنْعِمُ بالجلائل والدقائق، ومن جملة إنعامه : إنزاله القرآن وإيجادُهُ للخلائق. والأخير ـ وهو إيجاد الخلائق - دليل على سائر الصفات، والأول ـ وهو إنزالُ القرآن - دليل على السَّمْعِ والبصر والكلام.
And some of our teachers established the indication to the beliefs in another way: that the noun (al-ism) is general in the derived and non-derived; because what is meant by it is that which indicates the Essence alone, like 'Allah', or with consideration of an attribute, like 'the Knowing'. Whether permission to use it came literally as mentioned, or legally like 'the Maker', 'the Existent', 'the Necessary', for these three are established by consensus, and like 'the Speaker' based on the school of one who is satisfied with the occurrence of the [verbal] root. And the derived terms have their origin of derivation established for the one named by them. So 'Existent' indicates existence, 'Eternal' indicates pre-eternity, 'Everlasting' indicates permanence, 'Holy' indicates dissimilarity to contingents, 'Rich' indicates self-sufficiency, 'One' indicates oneness, 'Powerful' indicates power, 'Willing' indicates will, 'Knowing' indicates knowledge, 'Living' indicates life, 'Hearing' indicates hearing, 'Seeing' indicates sight, and 'Speaking' indicates speech. The [attributes] of meaning (al-ma'nawiyya) according to those who affirm them are clear from the intended meanings, and the impossible [attributes] are understood from the establishment of the mentioned attributes, and the possible [attributes] are understood from like 'Powerful' and 'Willing'. And ar-Rahman ar-Raheem: the Bestower of great and subtle blessings. And among His blessings are sending down the Quran and creating the creatures. The latter—creating the creatures—is evidence for the rest of the attributes, and the former—sending down the Quran—is evidence for hearing, sight, and speech.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of divine names and attributes. It begins by noting an alternative way some scholars have indicated the beliefs from the basmala: that the word 'name' (اسم) includes both derivative and non-derivative nouns. Derivative names like 'al-Qadir' (the Powerful) indicate an attribute (power), while non-derivative names like 'Allah' indicate the Essence alone. The author then lists a series of divine names and the attribute each one indicates: 'Mawjud' indicates existence, 'Qadim' indicates pre-eternity, etc. The list covers essential attributes (existence, pre-eternity, permanence, dissimilarity, self-sufficiency, oneness) and attributes of meaning (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech). Then it mentions that the attributes of meaning are clear from the names; the impossible attributes (like weakness) are understood as negated by the affirmation of the positive attributes; and the possible attributes (like creating) are understood from names like 'Powerful' and 'Willing'. Finally, the author connects two specific names—ar-Rahman and ar-Raheem—to the two great blessings: sending down the Quran and creating creatures. These two blessings serve as evidence: creation proves all attributes, and the Quran proves specifically hearing, sight, and speech (since it is a revelation that implies these).

Key Terms
اسم
مشتق
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine names are merely labels without indicating real attributes.
Correction: The passage establishes that derived names necessarily indicate attributes, as the derivation implies the attribute is present in the named.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a derived name like 'al-Knowing' indicate?
A) Only the Essence
B) The Essence plus an attribute
C) Only the attribute
D) Neither the Essence nor the attribute

Key Takeaway
Derived divine names are not arbitrary; they point to specific attributes that Allah possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as 'al-Knowing', do you truly believe that He knows every thought you have today? How would you live differently if you were completely convinced of this?
?
The name 'al-Wajib' (the Necessary) means Allah is the only being whose existence is necessary. In what area of your life have you been treating something else—like approval, wealth, or comfort—as if it were necessary for your happiness?
?
You have just learned that derived names like 'al-Maker' point to an attribute of Allah. How does this change the way you think about your own role as a 'maker'? Are you creating anything that contradicts the attribute of the true Maker?

Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
Divine Names and Their Indicated Attributes
Enumeration of divine names mapping to attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match thirteen divine names to the specific attributes they indicate.

Core Idea
Each divine name reveals a distinct attribute of Allah.

After establishing the principle that derived names indicate attributes, the author provides a concrete list of thirteen divine names and the attribute each one points to. This is a foundational list for understanding the Names and Attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of names and attributes
Go through each pair: name and the attribute it indicates. Notice that some attributes are essential (like existence, pre-eternity) and some are attributes of meaning (like power, will).
The list is in Arabic; try to recognize the pattern 'Name indicates attribute'.
2
Group the attributes
Identify which attributes are essential (sifāt dhātiyya) like existence, pre-eternity, etc., and which are attributes of meaning (sifāt ma'nawiyya) like power, will, knowledge.
Later the text will distinguish between meaning, impossible, and possible attributes.
3
Practice recall
Cover the attribute side and try to recall which name indicates which attribute, and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقَرَّرَ بعضُ مشايخنا الإشارة إلى العقائد بوجةٍ آخَرَ وهو أن الاسم عام في المشتق وغيره؛ لأن المراد به ما دَلَّ على الذات بمجردها كـ (الله)، أو باعتبار الصفة كالعالم، سواءٌ وَرَدَ الإذن به حقيقةً كما ذَكَره، أو حُكْماً كالصانع والموجود والواجب، فإن الثلاثة ثابتة بالإجماع، وكمُتَكَلَّم بناءً على مذهب من يكتفي بورود المادة، والمشتقاتُ ثَبَتَ مبدأ اشتقاقها لمن سُمّي بها، فموجود يدلُّ على الوجود، وقديم يدلُّ على القِدَمِ، وباقٍ يدلُّ على البقاء، وقُدُّوسٌ يدلُّ على المخالَفَةِ للحوادث، وغني يدلُّ على القيام بالنفس، وواحد يدلُّ على الوحدانية، وقادر يدلُّ على القدرة، ومُرِيدٌ يدلُّ على الإرادة، وعالِمٌ يدلُّ على العِلْمِ، وحي يدلُّ على الحياة، وسميع يدلُّ على السمع وبصير يدلُّ على البصر ومتكلّم يدلُّ على الكلام. والمَعْنَوِيَّة عند القائلين بها واضِحَةٌ من المعاني والمستحيلات مفهومةً من ثبوت الصفات المذكورة، والجائزات مفهومةٌ من نحو قادِرٍ ومُرِيدِ ، والرحمن الرحيم: المُنْعِمُ بالجلائل والدقائق، ومن جملة إنعامه : إنزاله القرآن وإيجادُهُ للخلائق. والأخير ـ وهو إيجاد الخلائق - دليل على سائر الصفات، والأول ـ وهو إنزالُ القرآن - دليل على السَّمْعِ والبصر والكلام.
And some of our teachers established the indication to the beliefs in another way: that the noun (al-ism) is general in the derived and non-derived; because what is meant by it is that which indicates the Essence alone, like 'Allah', or with consideration of an attribute, like 'the Knowing'. Whether permission to use it came literally as mentioned, or legally like 'the Maker', 'the Existent', 'the Necessary', for these three are established by consensus, and like 'the Speaker' based on the school of one who is satisfied with the occurrence of the [verbal] root. And the derived terms have their origin of derivation established for the one named by them. So 'Existent' indicates existence, 'Eternal' indicates pre-eternity, 'Everlasting' indicates permanence, 'Holy' indicates dissimilarity to contingents, 'Rich' indicates self-sufficiency, 'One' indicates oneness, 'Powerful' indicates power, 'Willing' indicates will, 'Knowing' indicates knowledge, 'Living' indicates life, 'Hearing' indicates hearing, 'Seeing' indicates sight, and 'Speaking' indicates speech. The [attributes] of meaning (al-ma'nawiyya) according to those who affirm them are clear from the intended meanings, and the impossible [attributes] are understood from the establishment of the mentioned attributes, and the possible [attributes] are understood from like 'Powerful' and 'Willing'. And ar-Rahman ar-Raheem: the Bestower of great and subtle blessings. And among His blessings are sending down the Quran and creating the creatures. The latter—creating the creatures—is evidence for the rest of the attributes, and the former—sending down the Quran—is evidence for hearing, sight, and speech.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of divine names and attributes. It begins by noting an alternative way some scholars have indicated the beliefs from the basmala: that the word 'name' (اسم) includes both derivative and non-derivative nouns. Derivative names like 'al-Qadir' (the Powerful) indicate an attribute (power), while non-derivative names like 'Allah' indicate the Essence alone. The author then lists a series of divine names and the attribute each one indicates: 'Mawjud' indicates existence, 'Qadim' indicates pre-eternity, etc. The list covers essential attributes (existence, pre-eternity, permanence, dissimilarity, self-sufficiency, oneness) and attributes of meaning (power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech). Then it mentions that the attributes of meaning are clear from the names; the impossible attributes (like weakness) are understood as negated by the affirmation of the positive attributes; and the possible attributes (like creating) are understood from names like 'Powerful' and 'Willing'. Finally, the author connects two specific names—ar-Rahman and ar-Raheem—to the two great blessings: sending down the Quran and creating creatures. These two blessings serve as evidence: creation proves all attributes, and the Quran proves specifically hearing, sight, and speech (since it is a revelation that implies these).

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah is 'Holy' (Quddus), completely unlike any created thing. When you think of Allah, do you ever subtly imagine Him in human terms—seeing, hearing, or acting like a powerful person? What mental image do you need to actively reject today to affirm His incomparability?
?
Allah is 'Rich' (Ghani), entirely self-sufficient. You, however, are desperately needy for Him in every breath. In what area of your life—finances, relationships, career—are you still acting as if you are self-sufficient, depending on your own planning rather than crying out to Him? What one practical step can you take today to shift that reliance?
?
Each divine name indicates a real attribute. If you truly believed that Allah is 'Seeing' (Basir) and 'Hearing' (Sami'), would you speak, think, or act differently in the next hour? What specific thought, word, or action do you need to change right now because He is watching and listening?

Attributes of Meaning, Impossible Attributes, and Evidence from Creation and Revelation
Classification of attributes and evidential basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the names and attributes lead to the classification of attributes into meaning, impossible, and possible categories, and how two great blessings serve as evidence for the attributes.

Core Idea
Creation and revelation are tangible proofs of Allah's attributes.

The author concludes the discussion by noting that the attributes of meaning are evident from the names, and that impossible attributes are understood as negated. Then he connects two specific names—ar-Rahman and ar-Raheem—to the great blessings of creating creatures and sending down the Quran, which serve as evidence for all attributes, especially hearing, sight, and speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the categories
The passage mentions three categories: attributes of meaning (ma'nawiyya), impossible attributes (mustahilat), and possible attributes (ja'izat). See how each is understood from the names.
The impossible are known through negation of the opposite, e.g., not being weak implies power.
2
Connect the names ar-Rahman and ar-Raheem to blessings
These two names indicate that Allah is the Bestower of great and subtle blessings. The author gives two specific blessings: sending down the Quran and creating creatures.
3
See the evidential role
The creation of creatures is evidence for all attributes (since it shows power, will, knowledge, etc.). The sending down of the Quran is specifically evidence for hearing, sight, and speech (since revelation implies that Allah hears, sees, and speaks).

Divine Attributes

Divine names indicate divine attributes
Al-Anbabi enumerates thirteen divine names and pairs each with its corresponding attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the thirteen divine names listed by al-Anbabi corresponds to a specific attribute of Allah according to Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Every divine name reveals a distinct attribute of Allah.

This passage from al-Anbabi's commentary on Umm al-Barahin lists the divine names and the attributes they indicate. It begins with a principle that derived names (al-mushtaqqat) have their basis established for the one named. Then it proceeds to pair each name with its corresponding attribute, covering the essential attributes of Allah according to the Ash'ari school. Understanding this connection helps us appreciate the relationship between Allah's names and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: a general principle followed by a list of name-attribute pairs.
The list is continuous; each name directly indicates an attribute.
2
Understand the principle
The opening statement 'wa al-mushtaqqatu thabata mabda' ishtiqaqiha...' means that each derived name has its root meaning established for the one who bears the name. This justifies why each name indicates a specific attribute.
Derived names are those formed from a root, like Qadir from qudra.
3
Identify each name-attribute pair
Go through the list and match each divine name with the attribute it indicates. For example, Mawjud indicates existence, Qadim indicates pre-eternity, etc. There are thirteen pairs in total.
The attribute 'mukhalafa lil-hawadith' is indicated by Quddus.
4
Reflect on the attributes
Notice that these thirteen attributes are central to Ash'ari theology: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike creation, self-subsistence, oneness, power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech.
These are among the 'sifat al-ma'ani' (attributes of meanings) in Ash'ari kalam.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to test yourself: which attribute does the name Qadir indicate? Answer: power (al-qudra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشتقاتُ ثَبَتَ مبدأ اشتقاقها لمن سُمّي بها، فموجود يدلُّ على الوجود، وقديم يدلُّ على القِدَمِ، وباقٍ يدلُّ على البقاء، وقُدُّوسٌ يدلُّ على المخالَفَةِ للحوادث، وغني يدلُّ على القيام بالنفس، وواحد يدلُّ على الوحدانية، وقادر يدلُّ على القدرة، ومُرِيدٌ يدلُّ على الإرادة، وعالِمٌ يدلُّ على العِلْمِ، وحي يدلُّ على الحياة، وسميع يدلُّ على السمع وبصير يدلُّ على البصر ومتكلّم يدلُّ على الكلام.
And the derived names have their derivation's basis established for the one who is named by them: so Mawjud (the Existent) indicates existence, Qadim (the Pre-eternal) indicates pre-eternity, Baqi (the Everlasting) indicates everlastingness, Quddus (the Holy) indicates being unlike the contingents, Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) indicates self-subsistence, Wahid (the One) indicates oneness, Qadir (the Powerful) indicates power, Murid (the Willer) indicates will, 'Alim (the Knowing) indicates knowledge, Hayy (the Living) indicates life, Sami' (the Hearing) indicates hearing, Basir (the Seeing) indicates sight, and Mutakallim (the Speaker) indicates speech.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of thirteen divine names from al-Anbabi's commentary, showing how each name is derived from an attribute and directly indicates that attribute. The author begins by stating a principle: derived names (al-mushtaqqat) are established for the one named by them based on their derivation. Then he proceeds to list the names, pairing each with the attribute it indicates. For example, 'Mawjud' indicates existence, 'Qadim' indicates pre-eternity, and so on up to 'Mutakallim' indicating speech. This list covers the essential attributes of Allah according to the Ash'ari school: existence, pre-eternity, everlastingness, being unlike creation, self-subsistence, oneness, power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. The passage emphasizes that the names are not arbitrary but are rooted in the attributes they signify.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call Allah 'al-Sami'' (the All-Hearing) but do you truly believe He hears every word you say—and even your silent thoughts? If He does, what words or thoughts do you need to repent of today? Will you watch your tongue for the next hour as an act of accountability?
?
Al-Quddus means Allah is unlike anything you have ever seen or imagined. Have you ever formed a mental image of Allah in your prayers or worship? Repent of that and commit to knowing Him only as He has described Himself in His names—starting now, without any image.
?
Allah is al-Ghani (Self-Sufficient), while you are completely dependent on Him for every breath. In what area of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient—your career, health, or relationships? Will you consciously pray for His help in that area today and admit your need?

Ism and Musamma: Ash'ari View and Sa'd's Critique
Anbabi presents the Ash'ari view that ism is identical to musamma, with proofs and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the name (ism) is identical to the named (musamma), the scriptural proofs cited, and al-Taftazani's critique of those proofs.

Core Idea
The identity of name and named is a bold Ash'ari claim with subtle scriptural backing—and equally subtle counterarguments.

This lesson examines a nuanced debate within the Ash'ari school about whether God's names are identical to His essence or distinct. Most Ash'aris argued for identity, citing Quranic verses and pre-Islamic poetry. However, the later scholar Sa'd al-Taftazani challenged this view by reinterpreting the same evidence. Let's explore the arguments and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with context
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three sections: al-Ajhuri's gloss, the Ash'ari claim with proofs, and al-Taftazani's response. Do not worry about every detail—just get the flow.
The passage is dense; it's okay to read it twice.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position and its evidence
Find where al-Anbabi states that most Ash'aris say 'the name is identical to the named.' Then note the three pieces of evidence: two Quranic verses (Surah al-A'la 1 and Surah Yusuf 40) and a line of poetry by Labid.
The phrase 'سَبِّحِ اسْمَ رَبِّكَ' is key.
3
Examine al-Taftazani's critique
Read al-Sa'd's response. He argues that the same verses actually imply a difference (al-mughāyara) between name and named. For each proof, note his alternative interpretation. For example, glorification might be of the name itself, not the named.
Look for the word 'اعتراف بالمغايرة'—'admission of difference'.
4
Reflect on the final counter-response
The passage ends with a possible rejoinder: the critique itself assumes a distinction between named entities and names, which is exactly what is being debated. Consider how this turns the tables.
This shows how theological arguments often circle back to first principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (وقوله: وَهُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ) يعني أن التحقيق أن المراد بلفظ الاسم: الأسماء الدالة على الذات الأقدس، ومعلوم أنها غيرُ الذات، وإنما كان هذا القول التحقيق؛ لأن لفظ الاسم حينئذ يكون مستعملاً فيما وضع له، خلاف ما إذا أريد به الذات. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ الْمُسَمَّى الحاصل أن أكثرَ الأَشَاعِرَةِ قالوا: الاسمُ عَيْنُ المسمّى بدليل قوله تعالى: (سَبِّحِ اسْمَ رَبِّكَ ) ، ﴿ مَا تَعْبُدُونَ مِن دُونِهِ إِلَّا أَسْمَاء ، وقول لَبِيد العامِرِيّ يخاطب ابنتيه في النَّيَاحَةِ عليه: فَقُومَا وَقُولَا بِالَّذِي تَعْرِفَانِهِ وَلَا تَخْمِشَا وَجْهاً وَلَا تَحْلِفَا شَعْرَا إِلَى الْحَوْلِ ثُمَّ اسْمُ السَّلَامِ عَلَيْكُمَا وَمَنْ يَبْكِ حَوْلاً كَامَلاً فَقَدِ اعْتَذَرَا قال السَّعْدُ في "شرح المقاصد": وفي الاستدلال بالآيتين اعتراف بالمغايرة حيث يقال: التسبيحُ والعبادة للذوات دون الأسماء، على أن التسبيحَ يَصِحُ لنفس الاسم بمعنى: تنزيهه عما ينافي التعظيم كما في البَيْضَاوِي، والعبادة تتعلق به ظاهراً لغرض الإشارة إلى أن هذه الآلهة عَدَمٌ في حضرة الألوهية، فكأنها أسماء لا مُسَمَّيات لها، ولفظ (اسم) في البيت مُقْحَم إشارةً إلى أنه ليس سلاماً حقيقياً؛ إذ هما لا يأمنان بعده. وقد يقال: لا اعتراف بالمغايرة في الاستدلال بالآيتين؛ لأن قولهم: التسبيح والعبادة للذات دون الأسماء على زَعْمِ القائل بأن هناك مسمياتٍ وأسماء.
The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri (His saying: 'and it is the verification') means that the verification is that intended by the word 'name' is the names indicative of the most holy essence, and it is known that they are other than the essence, and this saying is the verification only because the word 'name' then is used for what it was coined for, contrary to when the essence is intended. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi (His saying: 'that it is other than the named') — the summary is that most Ash'aris said: the name is identical to the named, evidenced by His saying Most High: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (Surah al-A'la, 1), and 'You worship not besides Him except names' (Surah Yusuf, 40), and the saying of Labid al-'Amiri addressing his daughters regarding lamentation over him: 'So stand and say by that which you know / And do not scratch a face nor tear hair / Until the year, then the name of peace be upon you both / And whoever weeps a full year has excused himself.' Al-Sa'd said in Sharh al-Maqasid: In inference by the two verses there is an admission of difference (al-mughāyara) since it is said: glorification and worship are for the essences, not the names — noting that glorification is valid for the name itself, meaning its sanctification from what contradicts veneration, as in al-Baydawi, and worship is attached to it outwardly for the purpose of indicating that these deities are non-existent in the presence of divinity, so they are as if names without named entities, and the word 'name' in the line of poetry is superfluous (muqḥam) indicating that it is not a real greeting, since they are not safe after it. And it may be said: There is no admission of difference in the inference by the two verses, because their saying: glorification and worship are for the essences, not the names, is based on the claim of the speaker that there are named entities and names.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a theological debate over whether the term 'name' (ism) is identical to the named (musamma) or distinct. It begins with al-Ajhuri's gloss confirming that the verification is that 'name' refers to the names of God, which are other than the essence. Then al-Anbabi notes that most Ash'aris hold that 'name' is identical to the named, citing scriptural proofs: two Quranic verses and a line from the pre-Islamic poet Labid. Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani (al-Sa'd) in his Sharh al-Maqasid critiques these proofs, arguing that they actually imply difference. For the first verse, glorification of the name of the Lord could be for the name itself, but it could also be interpreted as glorification of the named. For the second verse, worship of 'names' without named entities indicates they are mere names without realities. Labid's line uses 'name' superfluously to show the greeting is not real. The passage ends by suggesting that these criticisms themselves rely on the assumption that there are distinct named entities and names, which is the point under debate.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
الأشاعرة
التسبيح
العبادة
السعد
المقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume the Ash'ari position on name and named identity is universally accepted within the school.
Correction: The passage shows it is a majority view, but it was seriously challenged by later scholars like al-Taftazani, who reinterpreted the same proofs to argue for difference.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of most Ash'aris regarding the relationship between ism and musamma?
A) Ism is other than musamma
B) Ism is identical to musamma
C) Ism is a part of musamma
D) Ism and musamma are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view that name is identical to named is supported by Quran and poetry but subject to reinterpretation, highlighting the complexity of theological language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah' before an action, do you have a felt connection to the One named, or does the word remain a mere label? How can you deepen your awareness that the name is a window to the essence?
?
If the names of God are identical to His essence, how does that challenge your tendency to think of God's attributes as separate qualities? What would it mean to live as if every name you call upon is truly Him?
?
When you glorify God by saying 'Subhanallah,' do you sense that you are sanctifying the name itself, or only the being behind it? How does this debate reveal the risk of hollow words in your worship?

The Name 'Allah' as a Personal Proper Noun
Sanusi defines 'Allah' as a personal proper noun and explains its properness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name 'Allah' is considered a personal proper noun for God's essence and the two theories about how it came to be proper.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' is a unique personal proper name for God, not a description.

In this passage, Sanusi identifies the third name under discussion: 'Lafz al-Jalala' (the word of majesty, i.e., 'Allah'). He defines it as a proper name for God's essence, specifically a personal proper noun. The commentary clarifies that this properness can be understood in two ways: either as an original conventional proper name or as a proper name by predominant usage. Let's explore this foundational definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Sanusi's definition of 'Allah' as a personal proper noun.
Notice the phrase 'ala sabili alamiyyati al-shakhs'.
2
Distinguish the two theories of properness
The commentary explains two ways 'Allah' became a proper name: original designation vs. predominance. Identify each and how they differ.
3
Reflect on the teaching context
Sanusi notes that the term 'alam shakhsi' is only used in teaching. Consider why this caution is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث: لفظ الجلالة، وهو عَلَمٌ على ذاته تعالى على سبيل عَلَمِيَّة الشَّخص على التحقيق؛ وإن كان لا يجوز أن يقال ذلك إلا في مقام التعليم. وهو أشرفُ أسمائه تعالى بناءً على ما هو المختارُ من التفاوُتِ بينهما، ولذلك كان يقول سيدي علي وفا) في قوله تعالى: (وَكَلِمَةُ اللَّهِ هِيَ المُلْيَا ) (): هي لفظ الجلالة. وذهب بعضُهم إلى أنه لا تفاوُتَ بينهما ؛ لرجوعها كلّها إلى الذات المقدسة. وهو اسم الله الأعظم عند الجمهور، واختار النووي: أنه الحي القيوم. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري ومن قال: إن الاسم عين المسمى؛ يحمله على أن المراد أنه رأى الذات اهـ بزيادة يسيرة. ومنه تعلم أن الخلاف في كون الاسم عين المسمى أو غيره باعتبار مدلوله لا باعتبار نفسه. (قوله: عَلَى سَبِيل عَلَمِيَّةِ الشَّخْصِ أي العلمية المبتدأة بأن جعله الله علماً على ذاته ابتداء، فهو علم شخصي وضعي على التحقيق ومقابله أنه علم بالغلبة بأن وضع لمطلق المعبود بحق ثم غلب على الذات الأقدس من غير سبق استعمال في غيره، فهو على هذا علم بالغلبة التقديرية. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. لَكَ ذَاتُ الْعُلُومِ مِنْ عَالَمِ الْغَيْبِ وَمِنْهَا لِآدَمَ الْأَسْمَاءُ والتحقيق: أنه إن أُرِيدَ من الاسم: اللفظ؛ فهو غير مسماه قطعاً، وإن أريد به: ما يُفْهَمُ منه؛ فهو عَيْنُهُ، ولا فَرْقَ في ذلك بين جامد ومشتق (قوله: هِيَ لَفْظُ الْجَلَالَةِ) هذا خلاف ما عليه أهل الظاهر، وعبارة البيضاوي: المروجعل كلمة الذين كَفَرُوا السُّفْلَى ) يعني الشرك أو دَعْوَةَ الكُفْر، وَكَلِمَةُ الله هي العليا ام يعني: التوحيد أو دعوة الإسلام، والمعنى: وجَعَلَ ذلك بتخليص الرسول من أيدي الكفّار إلى المدينة، فإنه المبدأ له، أو بتأييده إياه بالملائكة
And the third: the word of majesty (Lafz al-Jalala), and it is a proper name for His essence, the Exalted, in the manner of a personal proper noun according to the investigation; although it is not permissible to say that except in a teaching context. And it is the noblest of His names, the Exalted, based on what is chosen from differentiation between them, and therefore Shaykh Ali Wafa used to say concerning His statement, the Exalted: 'And the word of Allah is the highest' (Quran 9:40): it is the word of majesty. And some went to that there is no differentiation between them, because they all return to the sacred essence. And it is the greatest name of Allah according to the majority, and al-Nawawi chose that it is al-Hayy al-Qayyum. Marginal gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: And whoever says that the name is the very same as the named, he understands it as meaning that he saw the essence, etc., with a slight addition. And from this you learn that the dispute over whether the name is the very same as the named or other is with regard to its referent, not with regard to itself. (His words: 'in the manner of a personal proper noun' means the original properness, in that Allah made it a proper name for His essence originally, so it is a personal conventional proper name according to investigation, and its opposite is that it is a proper name by predominance, in that it was placed for the absolute worshipped rightly and then predominated over the most sacred essence without prior usage for other than Him, so it is on this view a proper name by estimated predominance. Decisions of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. To You belongs the essence of sciences from the world of the unseen, and from them to Adam are the names. And the investigation: that if by 'name' is meant the word, then it is other than its named without doubt, and if by it is meant what is understood from it, then it is identical to it, and there is no difference in this between a solid noun and a derived one. (His words: 'It is the word of majesty') this is contrary to the position of the externalists, and the expression of al-Baydawi: 'and made the word of those who disbelieve the lowest' means polytheism or the call to unbelief, 'and the word of Allah is the highest' means monotheism or the call to Islam, and the meaning: and He made that by delivering the messenger from the hands of the polytheists to Medina, for it is the beginning for him, or by supporting him with angels.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a combination of Sanusi's matn and glosses. It first identifies the third name discussed: Lafz al-Jalala (the word of majesty, i.e., 'Allah'), and defines it as a proper name for God's essence, specifically a personal proper noun (alam shakhsi) in its origin, though this term is only used in teaching. It is the noblest of God's names because of the chosen view that there is differentiation in merit among God's names; Shaykh Ali Wafa interpreted the Quranic phrase 'the word of Allah is the highest' as referring to this name. Some scholars, however, hold that there is no differentiation because all names refer to the same sacred essence. The majority view is that 'Allah' is the greatest name, while Imam al-Nawawi held that 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' (the Living, the Self-Subsisting) is the greatest. The gloss by al-Ajhuri explains that the dispute about 'name' being identical to 'named' applies to the referent (madlul), not the word itself. Then al-Anbabi's notes clarify that 'alamiyyat al-shakhs' means the name was originally designated by God for Himself, not a name that became predominant through usage. The investigation (al-tahqiq) concludes that if 'name' means the word, it is distinct; if it means the understood meaning, it is identical, regardless of whether the word is a noun or derived. Finally, a gloss on 'Lafz al-Jalala' contrasts Sanusi's interpretation of the Quranic verse with that of al-Baydawi, who interprets it as referring to the call to Islam rather than the name itself.

Key Terms
لفظ الجلالة
علم
علمية الشخص
علم بالغلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the verified view (al-tahqiq) regarding how 'Allah' became a proper name?
A) It was originally a description that became predominant.
B) It was made a proper name by God originally (wad'i).
C) It is not a proper name at all.
D) It is a name by estimated predominance.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Allah' is a personal proper noun for God's essence, originally designated as such, not derived from common usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does knowing that 'Allah' is a personal proper name, not just a title, change the way you address Him in your prayers? Do you feel a deeper intimacy or awe?
?
If 'Allah' was originally designated by God Himself as His personal name, what does that imply about His desire to be known personally by you? How should that affect your effort to know Him?
?
When you say 'Allah', do you merely utter a label, or does it stir in your heart the reality that you are calling upon the unique Essence who has chosen this name for Himself? What keeps you from feeling that weight?

The Preeminence and the Greatest Name Debate
Sanusi describes the preeminence of 'Allah' and the debate around the greatest name, with commentary on name vs. named and a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'Allah' is considered the noblest name, the debate over differentiation among names, and the majority view that 'Allah' is the greatest name.

Core Idea
Allah is the noblest and greatest name, but scholars differ on whether names are equal in virtue.

Sanusi now explains the preeminence of the name 'Allah' over other divine names. He states that it is the noblest name, based on the chosen view that names differ in merit. Some scholars, however, argue for equality. The majority consider 'Allah' the greatest name, though al-Nawawi held a different opinion. The passage also addresses the dispute about whether the name is identical to the named and concludes with an interpretation of a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on nobility and the greatest name
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the claim that 'Allah' is the noblest name and the greatest name, and the supporting quotes.
2
Understand the disagreement on differentiation
Some scholars say names are equal because they all refer to the same essence. Identify this counterview and why Sanusi favors differentiation.
3
Examine the dispute about name vs. named
The passage clarifies that the dispute about 'name being identical to named' concerns the referent (meaning), not the word itself. This is resolved by distinguishing between the word and its meaning.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse interpretation
Shaykh Ali Wafa interprets 'the word of Allah is the highest' as referring to Lafz al-Jalala. Compare with al-Baydawi's interpretation mentioned in the gloss.
The verse is from Quran 9:40.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The name 'Allah' is the noblest and greatest name according to the majority, supported by Quranic verse, though some scholars differ on equality and the specific greatest name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the name 'Allah' is the noblest, does your speech reflect that reverence? When you say 'Allah' casually or in anger, are you desecrating the greatest name? What will you do to guard your tongue?
?
The passage clarifies that the name is not the same as the Named, yet the name points to Him. When you utter 'Allah', do you truly sense that you are referring to the Divine Essence, or is it merely a word? What practical step can you take to deepen that awareness?

The Name 'Allah': Proper Noun or Derived?
Al-Bajuri defines the name 'Allah', explains its status as a personal proper noun, and discusses debates on its derivation and relation to the named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the status of the name 'Allah' as a proper name for God, the debate over its derivation and nobility, and the distinction between name and named in theological discourse.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' is a unique proper name for God, distinct in meaning from its linguistic form.

Al-Bajuri begins by discussing the name 'Allah', the most revered of God's names. He clarifies its status as a personal proper noun and addresses key debates: whether it is derived, whether it is identical to God Himself, and whether it is the greatest name. This passage establishes foundational principles for understanding how we refer to God in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read its literal translation. Notice the various claims and arguments presented by Al-Bajuri.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the key debates
Look for the discussions on: whether 'Allah' is a personal proper noun or a name by predominance, whether it is the noblest name, whether it is the greatest name, and the relationship between name and named.
These debates are interconnected.
3
Understand the verification on name vs. named
Focus on the final part where Al-Bajuri presents the verification: if 'name' means the word, it is other than the named; if it means the meaning, it is identical.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

And the third is the word of majesty (لفظ الجلالة), and it is a proper name for His essence, exalted, on the basis of personal proper-noun-ness (علمية الشخص) truly; although it is not permissible to say that except in the context of teaching. And it is the noblest of His names, exalted, based on what is chosen of differentiation between them, and therefore Sayyidi Ali Wafa used to say regarding His statement, exalted: 'And the word of Allah is the highest' (Quran 9:40): it is the word of majesty. And some went to that there is no differentiation between them, because they all refer back to the sacred essence. And it is the greatest name of Allah according to the majority, and al-Nawawi chose that it is al-Hayy al-Qayyum. Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: And whoever said that the name is the named itself, he interprets it that the intended meaning is that he saw the essence, end, with a slight addition. And from it you learn that the disagreement concerning whether the name is the named itself or other than it is with respect to its meaning (مدلوله), not with respect to itself. His statement: 'on the basis of personal proper-noun-ness' means the original proper-noun-ness by which Allah made it a proper name for His essence from the beginning, so it is a personal proper name (علم شخصي) and a posited proper name truly, and its opposite is that it is a proper name by predominance (علم بالغلبة), in that it was posited for the absolutely worshiped rightly, then it predominated over the most sacred essence without prior usage in other than Him, so according to this it is a proper name by estimative predominance (علم بالغلبة التقديرية). And the verification is that if by 'name' is meant the word (لفظ), then it is other than its named certainly, and if by it is meant what is understood from it, then it is identical to it, and there is no difference in that between a non-derived and a derived name. His statement: 'it is the word of majesty' – this contradicts what the apparentists hold, and the expression of al-Baydawi: the...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses the name 'Allah' (لفظ الجلالة). The author states that 'Allah' is a proper name for God's essence, established as a personal proper noun (علمية الشخص) on the basis of verification, even though it may only be taught in a pedagogical context. He considers it the noblest of God's names, following the chosen view that names differ in rank. To support this, he cites 'Ali Wafa's interpretation of Quran 9:40 that 'the word of Allah' refers to the name 'Allah'. However, some scholars hold that no name is superior because all refer to the same essence. The passage then notes that 'Allah' is the Greatest Name according to the majority, though al-Nawawi preferred 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum'. The gloss of al-Ajhuri clarifies that the debate over whether the name is identical to the named (اسم عين المسمى) concerns the meaning (مدلول), not the word itself. The author then explains that 'Allah' as a personal proper noun means it was originally coined by God to refer to His essence, not just a name that became common ('علم بالغلبة') through predominant use. He presents the verification: if 'name' means the linguistic expression (لفظ), it is distinct from the named; if it means the concept understood, it is identical. This applies to both non-derived (جامد) and derived (مشتق) names. Finally, he notes that al-Baydawi's interpretation of 'the word of Allah' as 'the word of majesty' differs from the apparentist view.

Correction: The passage clarifies that 'Allah' is a personal proper name (not derived) and that the relationship between name and named depends on whether we mean the word or the meaning: the word is distinct, the meaning is identical.

The name 'Allah' is a proper name for God, but distinguishing its linguistic form from its conceptual meaning is crucial for theological precision.

Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim: The Beneficent Lord
Al-Bajuri defines the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim and explains their derivation from mercy as beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim and how they differ in scope while both denoting God's beneficence.

Core Idea
God's mercy means beneficence, not emotion — covering both great and small favors.

This passage explains two of the most frequently recited divine names, Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. Al-Bajuri clarifies that mercy in God's case means beneficence, not a feeling, and then distinguishes the two names by the type of favors they refer to. Understanding this distinction enriches our appreciation of the basmala and our supplications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own words settle before you interpret.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two attributes and their distinction
Notice that both names are derived from mercy but with a clarified meaning. Find how they differ: one for great favors, the other for delicate favors.
The key terms are جلائل النعم and دقائق النعم.
3
Understand why they are combined in the basmala
Reflect on the author's reason for combining them – what does it teach about how we should ask from Allah?
It encourages asking for both large and small needs.
4
Examine a common misconception
The passage guards against thinking that God experiences mercy as a tender feeling. Why is that impossible?
Emotions imply change and need, which are impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع والخامس الرحمن الرحيم، وهما صِفَتَان مأخوذتان من الرحمة، بمعنى: الإحسان في حقه تعالى؛ لأن معناها الأصلي ـ وهو رِئَةٌ في القلب تقتضي التفضَّلَ والإحسان - مستحيل في حقه تعالى فهما بمعنى: المُحْسِنِ، إلا أن الأوَّلَ بمعنى: المحسن بجلائل النِّعَمِ، والثاني: المُحْسِنِ بدقائق النعم، وإنما جَمَعَ بينهما إشارة إلى أنه تعالى كما ينبغي أن يُطلب منه النِّعَمُ العظيمةُ؛ ينبغي أن يُطْلَبُ منه النعم الحقيرة. ويتعلق بالبسملة أبحاث كثيرة، وفي هذا القدر كفاية.
And the fourth and fifth are al-Rahman and al-Rahim, and they are two attributes derived from mercy (الرحمة), in the sense of beneficence (الإحسان) with respect to Him, exalted; because its original meaning – which is a tenderness in the heart that necessitates favor and beneficence – is impossible with respect to Him, exalted. So both mean 'the Beneficent' (المحسن), except that the first means the Beneficent with great favors (جلائل النعم), and the second the Beneficent with delicate favors (دقائق النعم). And combining them indicates that He, exalted, just as it is fitting to ask Him for great favors, so it is fitting to ask Him for trivial favors. And many investigations relate to the basmala, and this much is sufficient.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the two names al-Rahman and al-Rahim. The author states they are both adjectives derived from 'mercy' (الرحمة), but the meaning of mercy for God is understood as 'beneficence' (الإحسان), because the original meaning of mercy – a tender feeling in the heart that motivates favor – is impossible for God (since He is not subject to emotions). So both names mean 'the Beneficent', but they differ in scope: al-Rahman refers to bestowing great favors (جلائل النعم), while al-Rahim refers to bestowing delicate favours (دقائق النعم). By combining them in the basmala, it indicates that one should ask God for both great and trivial favors. The author concludes by noting that many investigations relate to the basmala, but this much is sufficient for the present discussion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God experiences mercy as a tender emotion in His heart, similar to human compassion.
Correction: The passage explains that the original meaning of mercy (a tenderness in the heart) is impossible for God because He is not subject to emotions. Instead, mercy in God's case means beneficence (الإحسان), i.e., the act of bestowing favors.

Checkpoint

A) A feeling of tenderness in the heart.
B) Beneficence (الإحسان).
C) Forgiveness of sins.
D) Granting paradise.

Key Takeaway
Ar-Rahman denotes God's beneficence through great favors, while Ar-Rahim denotes His beneficence through delicate favors; both are derived from mercy understood as beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Understanding al-Rahman and al-Rahim
The commentator defines al-Rahman and al-Rahim and explains their distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the derivation and distinct meanings of the divine names al-Rahman and al-Rahim as explained in the Basmala.

Core Idea
Al-Rahman and al-Rahim both mean Benefactor, but differ in the scale of favors bestowed.

The Basmala begins with these two names, and understanding their precise meanings deepens our appreciation of Allah's mercy. The commentator explains that both names are derived from rahma but adapted to Allah's transcendence, and they teach us to seek both great and small favors from Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to how the commentator starts: 'And the fourth and fifth are al-Rahman and al-Rahim.'
2
Understand the derivation
Both names are derived from 'rahma' but their original meaning (a ventricle in the heart) is impossible for Allah. So they are used in the sense of 'beneficence' (ihsan).
The commentator explicitly states they mean al-Muhsin (the Benefactor).
3
Distinguish the two names
Al-Rahman is the Benefactor with great favors (jala'il al-ni'am), while al-Rahim is the Benefactor with subtle favors (daqa'iq al-ni'am).
This distinction shows that Allah's mercy covers everything from the grand to the minute.
4
Reflect on the wisdom of combining them
The combination indicates that both great and small favors should be sought from Allah, not considering anything too insignificant.
The commentator says: 'just as great favors should be sought from Him, so too should insignificant favors.'
