The Four Divisions of Praise and Allah's Worthiness
Enumerates four types of hamd and proves Allah's exclusive worthiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four divisions of praise (hamd) and the reason why Allah alone is worthy of all praise.

Core Idea
Allah deserves every kind of praise, from Himself, from creation, and among creatures.

This passage unpacks the meaning of 'al-hamdu lillah' by categorizing praise into four distinct types based on the relationship between the one praising and the one praised. The commentator then provides a logical argument showing that all these divisions ultimately point to Allah's exclusive worthiness of praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the four divisions, then explains the basis of Allah's worthiness.
The passage includes a gloss from al-Ajhuri.
2
Identify the four categories
List the four divisions: eternal from eternal, eternal from temporal, temporal from temporal, temporal from eternal. Understand each with examples.
3
Understand the reasoning for worthiness
The commentator argues about the object of praise: either a description of Allah or an act from Him. See how this covers all four divisions.
Even temporal praise of each other relates to Allah as the ultimate source.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this classification matters: it shows the comprehensiveness of Allah's worthiness and the dependency of all praise on Him.
This eliminates the misconception that creature-to-creature praise is independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الحمد لله أي الحمد بأقسامه الأربعة ـ التي هي: حمد قديم القديم وهو حمد الله نفسه بنفسه أزلاً، وحمد قديم لحادث، وهو حمد الله لأنبيائه وأوليائه، وحمد حادث لحادث، وهو حمد العباد بعضهم لبعض، وحمد حادث لقديم، وهو حمدنا الله - مستحق. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: (مستحق) وجه استحقاقه تعالى للأقسام كلها أن المحمود عليه؛ إما وصف له كما في حمده نفسه بنفسه وحمد الحوادث له أو واقع منه كما في حمده لأنبيائه وأوليائه وحمد الحوادث بعضهم بعضاً.
His saying: 'Praise be to Allah' means praise in its four divisions: praise of the eternal by the eternal, which is Allah's praise of Himself by Himself eternally; praise of the eternal by the temporal, which is Allah's praise of His prophets and saints; praise of the temporal by the temporal, which is people's praise of one another; and praise of the temporal by the eternal, which is our praise of Allah—[He is] worthy. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'worthy'—the aspect of His, may He be exalted, being worthy of all the divisions is that the object of praise is either a description of Him, as in His praise of Himself by Himself and the praise of temporal beings for Him, or an act originating from Him, as in His praise of His prophets and saints and the praise of temporal beings for one another.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of 'al-hamdu lillah' by dividing praise into four categories based on the relationship between the one praising and the one praised. The four are: (1) Eternal from Eternal: Allah's praise of Himself eternally; (2) Eternal from temporal: Allah's praise of His prophets and saints; (3) Temporal from temporal: people praising each other; (4) Temporal from Eternal: our praise of Allah. The commentator then explains that Allah is worthy of all these divisions because the praised quality either describes Him (as in His own praise) or is an action originating from Him (as in His praise of others, which returns to His grace), and even temporal praise of one another ultimately relates to Allah as the source of all good.

Correction: The passage shows that even temporal praise of one another ultimately falls under praise whose object is an act originating from Allah, because all good comes from Him.

The Five Pillars of Praise and Their Application to God
Sanusi lists the five pillars of hamd and explains their application when the Praised is God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five pillars of hamd (praise) and how Sanusi applies them to both human and divine contexts.

Core Idea
Praise has five essential pillars that apply to both human and divine praise.

Before discussing the attributes of God, Sanusi establishes the conceptual framework of praise itself. Understanding the five pillars of hamd clarifies how we speak about God's perfection. This passage enumerates these pillars, illustrates them with a human example, and then extends them to the unique case of God praising Himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five pillars
Read the opening sentence carefully. Sanusi lists five terms: hamid, mahmud, mahmud bihi, mahmud 'alayh, and sigha. Memorize them as the essential components of any praise.
These are the 'arkan' (pillars) — without them, praise is incomplete.
2
Work through the human example
Sanusi gives an example: praising Zayd for his generosity by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable.' Identify which element corresponds to each pillar in this example.
Notice that mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh can be different in essence or the same in essence but differ in consideration.
3
Observe the complication in divine praise
When God praises Himself, the hamid and mahmud are the same in essence (God) but differ in consideration (as praiser vs. praised). Similarly, mahmud bihi is reinterpreted as the attribute of perfection, and mahmud 'alayh as wisdom.
The sigha here is identified with God's eternal speech.
4
Understand the resolution
The author concludes that these pillars apply to both linguistic (human) and customary (divine) praise. If one restricts them to human praise only, the difficulty of applying them to God disappears.
This shows the flexibility of the framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأركانُ الحمد خمسة: حامد و محمود، ومحمود به، ومحمود عليه، وصيغة. فإذا حَمِدْتَ زيداً لكونه أكرمك مثلاً، كأن قلت زيد عالم؛ فأنت حامد، وزيد محمود، وثبوت العلم محمود به والإكرام محمود عليه، وقولك: زيد عالم صيغة. ثم إن المحمود به والمحمود عليه في هذا المثال اختلفا ذاتاً واعتباراً، وقد يتحدان ذاتاً ويختلفان اعتباراً، كأن يكون كلّ منهما الكرمُ، لكن من حيث كونه مدلول الصيغة يقال له : محمود به ومن حيث كونه باعثاً على الحمد يقال له: محمود عليه. (قوله: وَأَرْكَانُ الْحَمْد... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ أن هذه الأركان تجري في جميع الأقسام السابقة، ويمكن توجيهه بأن حَمْدَ القديم للقديم وُجِدَ فيه الحامد والمحمود، إلا أنهما مختلفان اعتباراً لا ذاتاً وإن لم يذكروا ذلك إلا في المحمود به، والمحمود عليه وهو مدلول الكلام الدال على الكمالات والمحمود عليه بمعنى: الحكمة لا الباعث، والصيغة: وهي نَفْسُ الكلام القديم، فالمراد بالصيغة في كلامهم الأمرُ الدال على التعظيم، فتَشْمَلُ: الكلام القديم، وتشمل أيضاً: عَمَلَ الأركان والجَنَان؛ إذ هذه الأركان ليست خاصة بالحمد اللغوي، بل تجري في العُرْفي على ما هو الظاهرُ. نعم؛ إن خُصَّتُ هذه الأركان بالحمد اللغوي الحادث لم يُختج لهذا التكلف، وانْدَفَعَ الإشكال.
And the pillars of hamd are five: the one who praises (hamid), the one praised (mahmud), that by which praise is [made] (mahmud bihi), that for the sake of which praise is [made] (mahmud 'alayh), and the form (sigha). So if you praise Zayd because he honored you, for example, as if you said 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then you are the praiser (hamid), Zayd is the praised (mahmud), the affirmation of knowledge is that by which praise is made (mahmud bihi), the honoring is that for the sake of which praise is made (mahmud 'alayh), and your saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the form (sigha). Then indeed mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh in this example differ in essence and consideration, but they may be united in essence and differ in consideration, such as when each of them is generosity, but insofar as it is the referent of the form it is called mahmud bihi, and insofar as it is the motivator for praise it is called mahmud 'alayh. (His saying: 'And the pillars of hamd... etc.') Its apparent meaning is that these pillars apply to all the previous divisions, and it is possible to reconcile it by saying that in the praise of the Eternal (al-Qadim) for the Eternal, there existed the praiser and the praised, except that they differ in consideration not in essence, even if they mentioned that only regarding mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh—and it (mahmud bihi) is the referent of the speech indicating perfections, and mahmud 'alayh means wisdom, not the motivator—and the form (sigha) is the very speech of the Eternal. So what is meant by 'form' in their speech is the matter that indicates glorification, so it includes: the eternal speech, and it also includes the action of the limbs and the heart; for these pillars are not specific to linguistic hamd, rather they apply in customary (urfi) praise as is apparent. Yes, if these pillars are restricted to contingent linguistic hamd, this effort is not needed and the difficulty is removed.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five pillars (arkan) of hamd (praise): the one who praises (hamid), the one praised (mahmud), that by which praise is expressed (mahmud bihi), that for the sake of which praise is given (mahmud 'alayh), and the form (sigha). The author illustrates with an example: if you praise Zayd for his generosity by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable', the components are identified. Then he notes that mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh may coincide in essence (like both being kindness) but differ in consideration (as referent vs. motivator). The commentary then addresses the application of these pillars to divine praise: when God praises Himself, the praiser and praised are the same in essence but differ in consideration. Mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh are interpreted as attributes of perfection and wisdom, not as a motivator. The form is identified with God's eternal speech. The author concludes that these pillars are not limited to linguistic hamd but also apply in customary (urfi) praise; if one restricts them to contingent human praise, the complication dissolves.

Key Terms

حامد
محمود
محمود به
محمود عليه
صيغة
حمد لغوي
حمد عرفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five pillars of hamd only apply to human praise and cannot be used when speaking of God's praise of Himself.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the pillars apply to divine praise as well, with appropriate adjustments in consideration (e.g., mahmud 'alayh is wisdom, not a motivator).

Checkpoint
In the example given, what is the 'form' (sigha) of praise?
A) Zayd's generosity
B) The act of praising
C) The statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable'
D) The intention of the praiser

Key Takeaway
The five pillars of hamd are universal, but when applied to divine praise, certain pillars (like mahmud bihi and mahmud 'alayh) take on a different consideration, such as wisdom instead of motivator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise God in your daily prayers, have you ever considered that you are fulfilling all five pillars? Which pillar do you think is weakest in your own praise—the sincerity of the form, or the awareness of why you praise Him?
?
Sanusi teaches that in God's self-praise, the 'mahmud 'alayh' is wisdom, not a benefit received. How does that challenge your own motives when you pray? Are you seeking something from God, or are you praising Him simply because He deserves it?
?
If the pillars of hamd apply not only to words but also to acts of worship and inner states, examine your heart: Is your obedience to God truly a form of praise? Or have you reduced worship to routine without the conscious intention of glorifying Him?

Applying the Pillars of Praise to Divine Praise
Al-Anbabi argues that the five pillars apply to divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five pillars of praise can be applied to God's praise of Himself without attributing human limitations.

Core Idea
The pillars of praise transcend human speech, reaching even divine self-praise.

This passage by Al-Anbabi tackles a subtle point: how can the five pillars of praise—praiser, praised, praise-object, praise-wisdom, and expression—apply when God praises Himself? Al-Anbabi argues that they do, but with a crucial distinction: the praiser and praised differ in consideration (iʿtibār) not in essence. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the meanings of each pillar in the divine context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by referencing a previous statement (qawluhu).
2
Identify the five pillars
The five pillars are: al-ḥāmid (praiser), al-maḥmūd (praised), al-maḥmūd bihī (that by which praise is given), al-maḥmūd ʿalayhi (that for which praise is given), and al-ṣīgha (expression). Note which ones are explicitly discussed and how they are adapted for divine praise.
The passage mentions all five but focuses on the latter three.
3
Understand the distinction between consideration and essence
In divine praise, the praiser and praised are the same entity (God) but differ in concept (iʿtibār). This resolves the apparent coincidence without compromising divine unity.
This is a key principle in kalām: conceptual distinctions within the divine essence.
4
Interpret the expression (ṣīgha) as eternal speech
Al-Anbabi clarifies that ṣīgha here is not a created utterance but the very eternal speech of God (al-kalām al-qadīm). This shifts the understanding from linguistic to customary praise.
Customary praise (al-ḥamd al-ʿurfī) includes actions of the heart and limbs, not just speech.
5
Reflect on the broader application
The passage concludes that these pillars are not limited to created human praise. If they were, there would be no need for this interpretation, but Al-Anbabi argues they apply more broadly.
This shows the flexibility of kalām in accommodating divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَرْكَانُ الْحَمْدِ... إلخ) ظاهرُهُ أن هذه الأركان تجري في جميع الأقسام السابقة، ويمكن توجيهه بأن حَمْدَ القديم للقديم وُجِدَ فيه الحامد والمحمود، إلا أنهما مختلفان اعتباراً لا ذاتاً وإن لم يذكروا ذلك إلا في المحمود به، والمحمود عليه وهو مدلول الكلام الدال على الكمالات والمحمود عليه بمعنى: الحكمة لا الباعث، والصيغة: وهي نَفْسُ الكلام القديم، فالمراد بالصيغة في كلامهم الأمرُ الدال على التعظيم، فتَشْمَلُ: الكلام القديم، وتشمل أيضاً: عَمَلَ الأركان والجَنَان؛ إذ هذه الأركان ليست خاصة بالحمد اللغوي، بل تجري في العُرْفي على ما هو الظاهرُ. نعم؛ إن خُصَّتُ هذه الأركان بالحمد اللغوي الحادث لم يُختج لهذا التكلف، وانْدَفَعَ الإشكال.
(His saying: 'And the pillars of praise... etc.') Its apparent meaning is that these pillars apply to all the previous divisions. And it can be directed that in the praise of the Eternal (al-Qadīm) for the Eternal, the praiser and the praised are found, but they differ in consideration (iʻtibār) not in essence (dhāt), even if they only mentioned that in al-maḥmūd bihī and al-maḥmūd ʻalayhi. And al-maḥmūd bihī is the meaning of the speech indicating perfections, and al-maḥmūd ʻalayhi means wisdom (al-ḥikma), not the motive. And al-ṣīgha is the very eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm). So what is meant by al-ṣīgha in their speech is the thing that indicates magnification, so it includes the eternal speech, and also includes the action of the bodily pillars and the heart. Indeed, these pillars are not specific to linguistic praise (al-ḥamd al-lughawī), but apply to customary praise (al-ḥamd al-ʻurfī) as is apparent. Yes, if these pillars were specific to created linguistic praise, there would be no need for this effort, and the problem would be removed.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a taqrīr by al-Anbābī commenting on the statement 'And the pillars of praise... etc.' He argues that the five pillars of praise apply to all previous categories of praise, including divine praise. In God's praise of Himself, we find the praiser (God) and the praised (God), but they differ in consideration (iʻtibār) not in essence. The author points out that the earlier discussion only mentioned the difference in maḥmūd bihī and maḥmūd ʻalayhi. Now he interprets that for divine praise, the maḥmūd bihī is the meaning of the speech indicating perfections, and the maḥmūd ʻalayhi is wisdom (al-ḥikma) rather than a motive (since God is not motivated by external causes). The expression (ṣīgha) is the very eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm) that denotes magnification. Therefore, the pillars are not exclusive to linguistic human praise (al-ḥamd al-lughawī al-ḥādith), but also apply to customary praise (al-ḥamd al-ʻurfī). If we restrict the pillars to human praise, the problem (ishkāl) of applying them to God disappears, but the author suggests the broader application is valid.

الكلام القديم

المحمود به
المحمود عليه
الصيغة

الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pillars of praise are only applicable to human, created speech and cannot be used to describe divine praise.
Correction: Al-Anbābī argues that the pillars apply to all categories of praise, including divine praise, when understood in terms of consideration (iʻtibār) and with the expression (ṣīgha) as eternal speech.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Anbabi, what is the 'ṣīgha' (expression) in the case of divine praise?
A) The created words of the prophet
B) The very eternal speech of God
C) The intention of the heart
D) The action of the limbs

Key Takeaway
The five pillars of praise apply to God's self-praise when the praiser and praised are distinguished by consideration (iʿtibār) not essence, and the expression (ṣīgha) is identified as the eternal speech of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Alhamdulillah', do you ever reflect that your praise is utterly dependent on God's own self-description? How does this realization affect the way you utter those words?
?
The lesson shows that in divine self-praise, the praiser and praised differ in consideration, not essence. Does this truth challenge any assumption you may have that your praise is somehow adding to or completing Allah's glory? What does it mean for your humility in worship?
?
If the pillars of praise apply to Allah's eternal speech itself, what weight do your own words of praise carry? Are they merely conventional, or do they participate in a deeper reality that demands sincerity and presence of heart? How will you change your practice of dhikr after this?

Eternal Praise as Eternal Speech
Sanusi identifies eternal praise with God's eternal speech via consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's eternal praise is not a separate attribute but is identical to His eternal speech when considered as indicating perfections.

Core Idea
Eternal praise is God's eternal speech viewed from one aspect.

This short but important note from Sanusi clarifies a potential confusion about divine praise. Since God's attributes are one in essence, terms like 'praise' refer to the same eternal speech under a particular consideration. Let's examine the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source once, noticing the key terms: الحمد القديم, الكلام القديم, بالاعتبار. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is a note (tanbih) that corrects a potential misconception.
2
Understand 'consideration' (iʿtibār)
The author explains that God's speech is one in essence but becomes multiple types by consideration. Reflect on how one reality can have many labels depending on the aspect we look at.
For example, commanding and forbidding are different considerations of the same speech.
3
Apply the lesson to divine attributes
Recognize that this principle – unity in essence, diversity in consideration – applies to all divine attributes. Eternal praise is not a separate entity but the very speech of Allah.
This guards against thinking that God has multiple eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينبغي التنبه له ـ كما قال بعضهم : أن الحمد القديم : هو الكلامُ القديمُ باعتبار دلالته على الكمالات؛ لأن الكلام القديم وإن كان واحداً بالذات؛ لكن يتنوّع بالاعتبار إلى أنواع كثيرة كما هو مشهور.
And among what one should pay attention to – as some of them said – is that eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) is the eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm) in consideration of its indication of perfections; because the eternal speech, even though it is one in essence, yet it diversifies by consideration into many types, as is well-known.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) reminding the reader that 'eternal praise' (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) is not a separate entity but is identical to God's eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm) considered from the aspect that it indicates divine perfections. The author explains that although God's speech is one in essence (wāḥid bi al-dhāt), it can be categorized into many types by consideration (bi al-iʻtibār). For example, when it is considered as commanding, it is called 'command'; when considered as indicating perfections, it is called 'praise'. This is a standard Ash'ari position that the divine attributes are not multiple in reality but are distinguished by mental considerations.

Key Terms
الحمد القديم
الكلام القديم
الدلالة
الكمالات
الاعتبار
الواحد بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal praise is a separate eternal attribute alongside God's speech.
Correction: Eternal praise is not a separate attribute; it is God's eternal speech under a specific consideration (indicating perfections).

Checkpoint
How can eternal praise (al-hamd al-qadim) be understood in relation to God's speech?
A) It is a separate eternal attribute
B) It is an action of God in time
C) It is God's eternal speech considered as indicating perfections
D) It is the praise of angels

Key Takeaway
Eternal praise is not a distinct attribute but the same eternal speech under a specific aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Alhamdulillah' in your prayer, do you realize that your praise is not merely your own words but an echo of God's eternal praise? How does this awareness transform your connection with that phrase?
?
If God's speech is one in essence yet diversifies into command, prohibition, and praise by mere consideration, what does that imply about the depth and richness of a single verse of the Quran? Can you approach each verse with the expectation of infinite meanings?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the reality of God's speech and our mental considerations of it. Does your worship ever treat God's attributes as separate entities rather than aspects of His single, transcendent essence? How can you unify your heart when you praise Him?

Eternal Praise is Eternal Speech
Al-Bajuri explains that eternal praise is eternal speech by indication of perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) is not a separate attribute but rather the eternal speech of Allah considered under the aspect of indicating His perfections.

Core Idea
Eternal praise is the eternal speech seen from a specific angle.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies a subtle point about the divine attribute of speech. When we say that God's praise is eternal, we are not referring to something distinct from His eternal speech; rather, it is that very speech considered in terms of what it expresses about God's perfections. This reflects the Ash'ari principle that God's speech is one in essence but diversified by consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the phrase 'باعتبار دلالته على الكمالات'.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that eternal praise is the eternal speech with respect to its indication of perfections. Find this claim and understand why al-Bajuri makes it.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Bajuri explains that eternal speech is one in essence but diversified by consideration (iʿtibār). Reflect on how the same attribute can have multiple names based on what it expresses.
This is a standard Ashʿarī point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينبغي التنبه له ـ كما قال بعضهم : أن الحمد القديم : هو الكلامُ القديمُ باعتبار دلالته على الكمالات؛ لأن الكلام القديم وإن كان واحداً بالذات؛ لكن يتنوّع بالاعتبار إلى أنواع كثيرة كما هو مشهور.
And among what should be attended to — as some of them said: that eternal praise (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) is the eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm) with respect to its indication of perfections; because eternal speech, even though it is one in essence, yet it is diversified by consideration into many types, as is well-known.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation. Al-Bajuri notes that one must be aware that the 'eternal praise' (al-ḥamd al-qadīm) mentioned in the basmala is not something separate from God's eternal speech; rather, it is that very eternal speech considered from the angle that it indicates God's perfections. He then gives the reason: although eternal speech is one in essence, it can be considered under different aspects (iʿtibārāt), resulting in many types, such as command, prohibition, information, and praise. This is a standard Ashʿarī point: God's speech is one eternal attribute, but it is called 'Qur'an', 'Torah', 'praise', etc., according to what it expresses.

Key Terms
الحمد القديم
الكلام القديم
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal praise is a separate eternal entity from eternal speech.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is the same eternal speech considered from the angle of indicating perfections.

Checkpoint

A) It is a separate attribute.
B) It is the eternal speech considered with respect to its indication of perfections.
C) It is a created thing made eternal.
D) It is the same as the basmala.

Key Takeaway
Eternal praise is not a separate attribute but the eternal divine speech considered from the perspective of its indication of God's perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Al-ḥamdu lillāh', do you truly believe that your praise is echoing an eternal reality, not merely a temporal act? Does this awareness change the weight of those words on your tongue?
?
If God's speech is one essence yet you read the Qur'an as command, story, and promise, are you seeking to encounter that single eternal speech behind every verse, or are you content with a superficial reading?

Objection and Answer on Privative Attributes
Al-Bajuri presents the objection and the most famous answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of the necessary being (wajib) is defended against the objection that it only covers affirmative existents, by reinterpreting 'non-existence' as the negation of the opposite.

Core Idea
Privations are covered because 'non-existence' means negation of the opposite.

In this passage, al-Bajuri addresses a subtle objection to the definition of the necessary being (wajib). The objector claims the definition only works for positive attributes, but the answer shows it actually includes privations by clarifying what 'non-existence' means. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that the definition of wajib (the mind cannot conceive its non-existence) only applies to affirmative existents, not to privations. Notice how he distinguishes between two types of attributes.
The objector distinguishes between wujūdī (affirmative) and ʿadamī (privative).
2
Understand the main answer
The answer reinterprets 'non-existence' to mean the negation of the opposite. For privations like pre-eternity (qidam), the mind cannot conceive the negation of that privation because that would affirm its opposite (ḥudūth).
This reinterpretation makes the definition applicable to privations.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this logic can be applied to other privative attributes. The answer shows the definition is comprehensive.
Think about other attributes like baqā' (eternality) or waḥdāniyya (oneness).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ أيضاً بأن التعريف لا يَصْدُقُ إلا بالواجب الوجودي كذاته تعالى، و موجوداتِ كمالاته الثبوتية - أعني: الأحوال -؛ إذ هذان لا يصدق العقل بعدمهما، دون العدمي - أعني : السُّلُوب - مع أن صدقه به هو المطلوب، فالتعريف غير جامع. وأجيب بأجوبة، منها - وهو أشهرُها -: أن المراد بعدمه: سَلْبُهُ ونَفْيُهُ بثبوت نقيضه، ولا شك أن السلوب كالقِدَمِ لا يَصدُقُ العقل بسلبها بثبوت نقيضها.
It was also objected that the definition applies only to the affirmative necessary being (al-wājib al-wujūdī) like His essence (dhātuhu taʿālā) and the existents of His positive perfections (kāmalātih al-thubūtiyya) – meaning the modes (aḥwāl) – for these two the mind does not assent to their non-existence, but not to the privative (al-ʿadamī) – meaning the negations (sulūb) – while its applying to it is what is required, so the definition is not comprehensive. And it was answered with several answers, among them – and it is the most famous – that what is meant by 'its non-existence' ('adamuhu) is its negation and denial by the affirmation of its opposite, and there is no doubt that the negations like pre-eternity (qidam) the mind does not assent to their negation by the affirmation of their opposite.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the definition of the necessary (wājib) and its most famous answer. The objector argues that the definition (that the mind cannot conceive non-existence) only holds for affirmative existents (wujūdī), such as God’s essence and His positive attributes (like knowledge, power) – these are attributes of perfection (kāmalāt thubūtiyya) and the so-called 'modes' (aḥwāl). For these, the mind cannot accept non-existence. However, the definition fails for privative attributes (ʿadamī or sulūb), such as ‘not having a beginning’ (qidam), ‘not having an end’ (baqā’), etc. Yet we also consider these attributes necessary for God, so the definition should include them. The answer (the most famous one) reinterprets 'its non-existence' ('adamuhu) in the definition to mean the negation of the opposite (salbuhu wa nafyuhu bi-thubūt naqīḍihi). For privations like qidam (pre-eternity), the mind cannot assent to the negation of qidam (i.e., it cannot think of God as originated) because that would affirm the opposite (ḥudūth). Thus the definition applies to privations as well, because the mind cannot accept the non-existence of the privation (i.e., cannot think that God is not eternal) by affirming its opposite. The answer uses the same logic: the inability to conceive the negation of a necessary privation means the mind cannot affirm the opposite quality.

Key Terms
وجودي
عدمي
سلوب
أحوال
قدم
نقيض
جامع
اعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of wajib only works for positive attributes like God's essence and His positive perfections.
Correction: The definition also covers privative attributes like pre-eternity (qidam) because 'non-existence' in the definition means the negation of the opposite; for privations, the mind cannot conceive their negation (which would affirm the opposite).

Checkpoint
What is the most famous answer to the objection that the definition of wājib does not cover privative attributes (sulūb)?
A) The definition only applies to God's essence, not His attributes.
B) The term 'non-existence' in the definition means the negation of the opposite.
C) Privative attributes are not real attributes and thus need not be covered.
D) The definition was revised to include both types.

Key Takeaway
The definition of wajib applies to both positive and privative attributes by interpreting 'non-existence' as the negation of the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that some of God's attributes are actually negations of imperfections, such as His being without a beginning? How does this shape your awe of His absolute perfection?
?
If the definition of God's necessity requires that you cannot possibly conceive of Him being otherwise, are there moments when your heart or actions treat His attributes as optional or negotiable?
?
This lesson says that for God's pre-eternity, the mind cannot affirm its opposite. Is there any divine attribute you have mentally questioned or allowed doubt about? What would it take to bring your certainty in line with this logical necessity?

The Meaning of 'Fi Haqqi Mawlana' and Attributes of Jalal and 'Izza
Sanusi explains the phrase 'fi haqqi mawlana' and the terms jalla and azza.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the phrase 'fi haqqi mawlana' and the distinction between the attributes of jalal (majesty) and 'izza (might) as categories of negative and positive attributes.

Core Idea
God is described by negating imperfections and then affirming perfections.

This lesson unpacks the opening phrase of many Islamic theological texts: 'fi haqqi mawlana' and the two epithets 'jalla' and 'azza'. Sanusi clarifies the linguistic nuances and shows how these terms point to two fundamental categories of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to get the flow. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the definitions of jalla and azza.
2
Understand the phrase 'fi haqqi mawlana'
Notice how Sanusi explains each word: 'fi' as 'laam', 'haqq' as the reality/essence, 'mawla' as Helper or Manager.
3
Grasp the two attributes
See that jalla refers to negation of imperfections (sifaat al-salb) and azza refers to affirmation of perfections (sifaat al-thuboot).
The order jalla then azza is intentional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فِي حَقِّ مَوْلَانَا) (في) بمعنى اللام والحق بمعنى: الحقيقة التي هي الذات. والمَوْلى يُطْلَقُ على معان كثيرة، المناسِبُ منها الناصِرُ، والأنسب المُتَوَلّي أمورنا.
(قوله: جَلَّ) أي: تنزّة عما لا يَليقُ به فمَرْجِعُ الجلالة إلى صفات السلب (وَعَنَّ أي: اتَّصَفَ بما يليقُ به فمرجِعُ العِزَّة إلى صفات الثبوت، وعلى هذا يكون تقديمُ (جل) على (عز).
His saying: 'In the right of our Lord (fi haqqi mawlana)'. 'Fi' is in the sense of 'laam' (for), and 'haqq' is in the sense of the reality (haqiqah) which is the essence (dhaat). 'Mawla' is applied to many meanings; the appropriate among them is the Helper (al-naasir), and more appropriate is the One who manages our affairs (al-mutawalli umurana).

His saying: 'Jall (Exalted)' i.e., He is transcendent above what is not befitting Him, so the source of jalaalah (majesty) refers to negative attributes (sifaat al-salb). And 'Azza (Almighty)' i.e., He is described with what is befitting Him, so the source of 'izzah (might) refers to positive attributes (sifaat al-thuboot). On this basis, the precedence of 'jall' over 'azza' [is understood].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage provides a philological and theological explanation of the phrase 'fi haqqi mawlana' (in the right of our Lord) and the divine epithets 'jalla' and 'azza'. The author first notes that 'fi' here functions like the preposition 'laam' (meaning 'for' or 'pertaining to'), and 'haqq' means the reality, which is the essence (dhaat). The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings, but the most appropriate in this context is 'al-naasir' (the Helper) or more fittingly 'al-mutawalli umurina' (the One who manages our affairs). Then he explains 'jalla' (Exalted): it signifies that God is transcendent (munazzah) above all that is not befitting Him, and thus the attribute of jalaalah (majesty) pertains to the negative attributes (sifaat al-salb)—those that negate imperfections. On the other hand, 'azza' (Almighty) signifies that God is described with what befits Him, so 'izzah (might) refers to the positive attributes (sifaat al-thuboot)—those that affirm perfections. The precedence of 'jalla' over 'azza' in the common formula is thus based on the priority of negating imperfections before affirming perfections.

Key Terms
الحق
الذات
المولى
جل
عز
صفات السلب
صفات الثبوت
الجلالة
العزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: jalla and azza are synonymous
Correction: jalla refers to negative attributes (transcendence), while azza refers to positive attributes.

Checkpoint
To which category of divine attributes does 'jalla' (Exalted) refer according to the passage?
A) Positive attributes (sifaat al-thuboot)
B) Negative attributes (sifaat al-salb)
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The epithets 'jalla' and 'azza' respectively negate imperfections and affirm perfections, establishing the two foundational categories of divine attributes in Sunni theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you first clear your mind of unworthy thoughts (tanzih) before affirming His perfections? If not, what does this say about your understanding of His majesty?
?
The phrase 'fi haqqi mawlana' means 'in the right of our Lord' – do you truly live as if your Lord is the One managing all your affairs, or do you still feel you control your own life?
?
Knowing that 'jalla' negates all imperfections from Allah, what specific false beliefs about Allah do you need to actively negate in your heart today?

Obligatory Knowledge: Detailed and Summary
Al-Bajuri distinguishes between detailed and summary obligatory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that obligatory knowledge about God is divided into two categories: matters that must be known in detail (the twenty essential attributes) and matters that suffice with summary knowledge (all other perfections and impossibilities).

Core Idea
Knowing God is obligatory, but some truths demand detailed understanding while others need only general affirmation.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying the scope of obligatory knowledge. He explains that while knowing God is required of every Muslim, the level of detail varies depending on the availability of proofs. This passage establishes the foundational principle that some attributes must be known in detail, while others are sufficient in summary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
Read the Arabic passage once. Then look for the two types of obligatory knowledge: that which requires detailed knowledge and that which suffices with summary knowledge.
Notice the use of 'تفصيلاً' and 'إجمالاً'.
2
Understand the basis for detail vs summary
The distinction is based on the nature of the proofs: detailed proofs exist for the twenty essential attributes, while summary proofs exist for the rest of God's perfections and impossibilities.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'فتأمل' (so reflect)
Al-Bajuri ends this section with 'فتأمل', inviting the reader to ponder the wisdom behind this division.
Reflection is a key part of Islamic learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: مَا يَجِبُ... إلخ) أي: جميع ما يجبُ إلخ؛ لأن (ما) من صيغ العموم، لكن ما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً - وهو العشرون الآتية -؛ يَجِبُ على المكلف أن يعرفه كذلك ـ أعني: تفصيلاً ـ، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالاً ـ وهو سائر الكمالات - ؛ يجب على المكلف أن يعرفه كذلك ـ أعني: إجمالاً -، وكذلك يُقَال فيما يستحيلُ. فتأمل. (قوله: فِي حَقِّ مَوْلَانَا) (في) بمعنى اللام والحق بمعنى: الحقيقة التي هي الذات. والمَوْلى يُطْلَقُ على معان كثيرة، المناسِبُ منها الناصِرُ، والأنسب المُتَوَلّي أمورنا. (قوله: جَلَّ) أي: تنزّة عما لا يَليقُ به فمَرْجِعُ الجلالة إلى صفات الشلوب (وَعَنَّ أي: اتَّصَفَ بما يليقُ به فمرجِعُ العِزَّة إلى صفات الثبوت، وعلى هذا يكون تقديمُ (جل) على (عز) من باب تقديم التخلية على التحلية. وقيل غير ذلك. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: مِنْ بَابِ تَقْدِيمِ التَّخْلِيَةِ ... إلخ) فيه نوع بَشَاعَةٍ، وقال بعض مشايخنا: لا تتوهم أن المُخَلَّى المحلّى: الحقُّ، بل الخلق تتخلى نفوسهم من العقد الزائغ بعد ذكر المحبوب، ثم تتحلى بالعقد الصحيح.
(His statement: 'what is obligatory... etc.') meaning: everything that is obligatory etc.; because (ma) is one of the forms of generality, but that for which the intellectual or transmitted proofs exist in detail—and it is the coming twenty—it is obligatory for the morally responsible person to know it likewise, I mean: in detail. And that for which the intellectual or transmitted proofs exist in summary—and it is the rest of the perfections—it is obligatory for the morally responsible person to know it likewise, I mean: in summary. And likewise it is said concerning what is impossible. So reflect. (His statement: 'concerning our Master') ('fi' is in the sense of 'li' [belonging to] and 'haqq' is in the sense of: the reality which is the essence. And 'al-mawla' is applied to many meanings, the appropriate among them is the helper, and the more appropriate is the one who manages our affairs. (His statement: 'Jalla') meaning: He is transcendent above what is not befitting Him, so the reference of 'al-jalalah' is to the attributes of negation (sifat al-salb). (And 'Azza') meaning: He is described with what is befitting Him, so the reference of 'al-izzah' is to the attributes of affirmation (sifat al-thubut). And on this basis, the precedence of 'Jalla' over 'Azza' is from the chapter of giving priority to purification (takhliyah) over adornment (tahliyah). And other than that is said. Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'from the chapter of giving priority to purification... etc.') in it there is a kind of repulsiveness (basha'ah). And some of our teachers said: Do not imagine that the one purified and adorned is the Truth (al-Haqq); rather, the creatures purify their souls from the deviant creed after mentioning the Beloved, then they adorn themselves with the correct creed.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of obligatory knowledge concerning God. It first explains that 'what is obligatory' (ma yajib) is general, but it is divided into two kinds: those obligatory matters for which there are detailed proofs—the twenty essential attributes which will be listed—must be known in detail by every morally responsible person (mukallaf). The rest of God's perfections (sair al-kamalat) need only be known in summary, as they are proven by summary proofs. The same applies to what is impossible for God (mustahil). Then the passage explains the phrase 'fi haqq mawlana' (concerning our Master). 'Fi' here means 'li' (belonging to), and 'haqq' means the reality or essence. 'Mawla' has several meanings; the appropriate one here is 'helper' (nasir) or more specifically 'the one who manages our affairs' (al-mutawalli umurina). Next, 'Jalla' indicates God's transcendence (tanzih) above all that is unbefitting, and thus refers to the negative attributes (sifat al-salb). 'Azza' indicates that God is described with what befits Him, referring to the positive attributes (sifat al-thubut). The precedence of 'Jalla' over 'Azza' is like giving priority to purification (takhliyah) over adornment (tahliyah)—first negating imperfections, then affirming perfections. The marginal note warns against misunderstanding: the purification and adornment are not applied to God but to the believer's soul, which must first purify itself from false beliefs before adorning itself with correct beliefs.

Key Terms
ما يجب
تفصيلاً
إجمالاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All obligatory knowledge about God must be known in detail.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the twenty essential attributes require detailed knowledge; the rest of the perfections and impossibilities can be known in summary.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what is the basis for dividing obligatory knowledge into detailed and summary?
A) The number of attributes
B) The type of proof (detailed vs summary)
C) The personal preference of the scholar
D) The level of the student

Key Takeaway
Obligatory knowledge is not monolithic; some attributes require detailed study, while others are known summarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about God's attributes, do you tend to focus only on the famous twenty and neglect the countless other perfections of Allah? How does this lesson challenge you to expand your understanding? This week, commit to pondering one of those 'summary' attributes and let it transform your worship.
?

?
The word 'فتأمل' (so reflect) at the end of the passage is a command. Will you obey it? Take five minutes right now to reflect on at least one attribute of Allah that you have only known in summary until now. What does that attribute demand of you personally?

The Meaning of 'Jalla' and 'Azza'
Al-Bajuri explains the terms 'Jalla' and 'Azza' and their relation to negative and positive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological significance of the terms 'Jalla' (He is transcendent) and 'Azza' (He is mighty), and why the order of these terms reflects the priority of negation over affirmation.

Core Idea
'Jalla' removes all imperfections; 'Azza' affirms all perfections—negation must come first.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri explains the common divine epithets 'Jalla' and 'Azza' that appear in Islamic texts. He shows that these are not mere honorifics but carry deep theological meaning, representing the two categories of divine attributes: those of negation (sifat al-salb) and those of affirmation (sifat al-thubut). The marginal note clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meanings of 'Jalla' and 'Azza'
Read the definitions: 'Jalla' means He is transcendent above what is not befitting; 'Azza' means He is described with what befits Him.
2
Relate them to negative and positive attributes
Notice that 'Jalla' corresponds to the attributes of negation (sifat al-salb) and 'Azza' corresponds to the attributes of affirmation (sifat al-thubut).
These two categories cover all divine attributes.
3
Grasp the order: purification before adornment
The order 'Jalla then 'Azza' is deliberate: it follows the principle of takhliyah (purification) before tahliyah (adornment), meaning one must first negate imperfections before affirming perfections.
The marginal note warns that this purification and adornment apply to the believer's soul, not to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson teaches that we purify our souls from deviant creed *after mentioning the Beloved*. What specific deviant belief about God's attributes—perhaps one you inherited from culture or upbringing—do you need to uproot from your heart today, starting with this very mention of Allah?

Detailed vs. Summary Knowledge of God's Impossibilities and Possibilities
Al-Bajuri explains the categories of impossible and possible regarding God with the tafsili/ijmal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri distinguishes between detailed and summary knowledge regarding what is impossible and possible for God, and the role of the twenty opposites and other deficiencies.

Core Idea
Some impossibilities about God must be known in detail; others suffice in summary.

In this passage, al-Bajuri applies the same distinction between detailed (tafsili) and summary (ijmali) knowledge to the categories of what is impossible (mustahil) and what is possible (ja'iz) for God. He explains that the twenty opposites—the impossibilities corresponding to the essential attributes—must be known in detail, while other deficiencies need only summary knowledge. He also notes an ellipsis in the text regarding 'what is possible'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the mention of 'twenty opposites' and 'rest of deficiencies'.
The text uses 'ma' as a generality particle.
2
Identify the two knowledge categories
Understand that what is impossible is divided into two: those with detailed proofs (the twenty opposites) requiring detailed knowledge, and other deficiencies requiring only summary knowledge. Same applies to what is possible.
3
Understand the ellipsis (hadhf)
Note that the text has an ellipsis regarding 'what is possible' because it is indicated by the previous discussion. This is a stylistic feature.
The ellipsis does not change the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ) أي: في حق مولانا جل وعز، وكذلك يقال في قوله: (وما يجوز، ففيه الحذفُ من غير الأول لدلالته عليه، وقد علمت أن المراد: جميع ما يستحيل؛ لأن (ما) من صيغ العموم، لكن ما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً - وهو العشرون الأضداد الآتيةُ ؛ يجب على المكلَّف أن يعرفَهُ كذلك - أعني: تفصيلاً ، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالاً - وهو سائر النقائص -؛ يجب المكلَّف أن يعرفه كذلك - أعني : إجمالاً ـ كما تقدم التنبيه عليه. (قوله: وَمَا يَجُوزُ أي: في حق مولانا جل وعز كما علمت
(His statement: 'and what is impossible') i.e. concerning our Master, Glorified and Exalted, and likewise it is said in His statement: ('and what is possible', and in it there is ellipsis (hadhf) from the first [of the two] because the first indicates it. And you have known that the meaning is: all that is impossible; because (ma) is one of the forms of generality, but that for which the intellectual or transmitted proofs exist in detail—and it is the coming twenty opposites—it is obligatory for the morally responsible person to know it likewise, I mean: in detail. And that for which the intellectual or transmitted proofs exist in summary—and it is the rest of the deficiencies—it is obligatory for the morally responsible person to know it likewise, I mean: in summary, as has been previously noted. (His statement: 'and what is possible') i.e. concerning our Master, Glorified and Exalted, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage extends the same analysis to what is impossible (mustahil) and what is possible (ja'iz) for God. It first clarifies that 'what is impossible' is general (using 'ma' as a generalizing particle), but it is divided into two categories: those impossibilities for which there are detailed proofs—the twenty corresponding opposites of the attributes—must be known in detail by the mukallaf. The rest of the deficiencies (sair al-naqa'is) need only be known in summary. Then it mentions that 'what is possible' (ja'iz) is similarly understood in the context of God, although the text has an ellipsis (hadhf) because it is indicated by the previous discussion. The passage reiterates that the same rule of detailed vs. summary knowledge applies to all three categories (obligatory, impossible, possible) for God, as was previously pointed out.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'twenty opposites' are additional positive attributes of God.
Correction: The twenty opposites are the negations (impossibilities) corresponding to the twenty essential attributes, not positive attributes.

Key Takeaway
The believer must know the twenty opposites (impossibilities corresponding to God's attributes) in detail, while other deficiencies about God can be known in summary.

Definition of Sifa and the Classification of Attributes and Things
Al-Bajuri defines 'sifa', lists twenty attributes, and explains the four categories of things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Bajuri defines the term 'attribute' (sifa) and distinguishes three types of attributes, and how this classification connects to the broader division of things into existents, non-existents, and states.

Core Idea
Attributes are not only existential qualities; they include states and non-existential concepts too.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying the technical meaning of 'sifa' in kalam, distinguishing it from the essence. He then categorizes the twenty attributes into existential, state, and non-existential types. Finally, he explains that this classification rests on the doctrine of states, which divides all things into existents, non-existents, and states—a foundational framework for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall sense.
Notice the technical terms: sifa, wujudi, hal, adami.
2
Identify the three types of attributes
From the passage, list the three types: existential (wujudi), state (hal), and non-existential (adami). Give an example for each from the text.
Power and will are existential; being powerful and being willing are states; eternity and everlastingness are non-existential.
3
Understand the four categories of things
The passage says that the classification of attributes is based on the doctrine of states, which holds that things are four categories: existents (can be seen) and non-existents (no establishment). Note the marginal note that further divides non-existents into pure non-existents and non-existential concepts.
This is a nuanced point: non-existential concepts like God's everlastingness are not pure nothingness.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it allows kalam to speak about God's attributes in a precise way, avoiding anthropomorphism and affirming real distinctions.
The doctrine of states is a key Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً تُطْلَقُ الصّفَةُ على : المعنى الوجودي القائم بالموصوف، :وعلى ما ليس بذاتٍ، وهذا هو المراد هنا؛ لأن هذه العشرين منها ما هو وجودي كالقُدْرَة والإرادةِ، ومنها ما هو حال كالكَوْنِ قادراً والكونِ مُرِيداً، ومنها ما هو عَدَمِيٌّ كالقِدَمِ والبقاء، وما ذَكَرَهُ المصنف من أن الواجب التفصيلي عشرون صفةً، والمستحيل التفصيلي كذلك؛ مبني على القول بثبوت الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء أربعة أقسام موجودات وهي ما تَصِحُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، ومعدومات: ما لا تُبُوتَ له. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري بأَنَّ الْأَشْيَاءَ أي الأمور الثابتة في الواقع ونفس الأمر. (قوله: (وَمَعْدُومَاتٌ المراد بها ما يفسر بالعدم كالقدم المفسر بعدم الأولية، تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَمَعْدُومَاتٌ : وَهِيَ مَا لَا تُبُوتَ (لَهُ أي ليس له تحقق في نفسه، أو له تحقق لكن مفهومه عدميّ، فشَمِلَتِ المعدوماتُ : المعدوماتِ المَحْضَةَ، كابن زيد في حالة عدمه، والعدميات كبقاء الله ،وقدّمه وإلا وَرَدَ عليه أن الأشياء خمسةٌ بزيادة العدميات تأمل.
Twenty attributes. The term 'attribute' (sifa) is applied to: the existential meaning subsisting in the described, and to what is not an essence, and this is what is intended here; because among these twenty, some are existential like power and will, some are states (hal) like being powerful and being willing, and some are non-existential (adami) like eternity and everlastingness. And what the author mentioned—that the detailed obligatory (attributes) are twenty attributes, and the detailed impossible (attributes) likewise—is based on the doctrine of the establishment of states (ahwal), which is based on the way that says that things are four divisions: existents (mawjudat)—which are what can be seen; and non-existents (ma'dumat)—which have no establishment. Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: that 'things' (al-ashya') means matters established in reality and in the matter itself. (His saying: 'and non-existents'—what is intended by them is what is interpreted as non-existence, like eternity interpreted as having no beginning. Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'and non-existents'—they are what have no establishment, i.e., they have no actuality in themselves, or they have actuality but their concept is non-existential. Thus, non-existents include: pure non-existents, like 'son of Zayd' in the state of his non-existence, and non-existential things (adamiyyat) like the everlastingness of God, and he gave priority to it; otherwise it would be objected that things are five with the addition of non-existential things. Reflect.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage. Al-Bajuri defines the term 'attribute' (sifa) as applied to an existential meaning subsisting in the described, or to what is not an essence. The twenty attributes that require detailed knowledge are of three types: existential (wujudi), like power and will; states (hal), like being powerful and being willing; and non-existential (adami), like eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa). The author's statement that the detailed obligatory attributes are twenty, and the detailed impossible are also twenty, is based on the doctrine of states (ahwal), which holds that things are divided into four categories: existents (which can be seen) and non-existents (which have no establishment). The marginal notes clarify that 'things' means matters established in reality, and that 'non-existents' include both pure non-existents (like 'son of Zayd' before birth) and non-existential concepts (like the everlastingness of God, which is interpreted as having no end). The note warns that if non-existential concepts are considered separate, then things would be five categories. This illustrates the nuanced classification of attributes in Sunni kalam.

Correction: The passage shows that attributes also include states (like being powerful) and non-existential attributes (like eternity), which are not independent entities but necessary concomitants of God.

Key Takeaway
In Sunni kalam, 'sifa' includes existential attributes, states, and non-existential concepts, and this classification is grounded in the division of all things into existents, non-existents, and states.

?

Definition of Sifa and Classification of the Twenty Attributes
Defines 'sifa' and classifies twenty attributes into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'sifa' and the three-fold classification of the twenty divine attributes into existential, state, and negative.

Core Idea
Attributes are not all existential; some are states, some are negative.

Before we explore the specific attributes, we need to clarify what the term 'attribute' (sifa) means in this context. Al-Bajuri explains that 'sifa' can refer to an existential meaning residing in the described, or to something that is not the essence itself. This distinction is crucial because among the twenty attributes, some are existential (like power), some are states (like being powerful), and some are negative (like pre-eternity). This lesson establishes the foundational classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how the author first defines 'sifa' and then applies it to the twenty attributes.
Pay attention to the terms 'wujudi', 'hal', and 'adami'.
2
Identify the three types of attributes
From the passage, extract the three categories: existential (wujudi), state (hal), and negative (adami). Give an example of each from the text.
Power and will are existential; being powerful and being willing are states; pre-eternity and everlastingness are negative.
3
Understand the basis in the theory of states
The passage mentions that this classification relies on the theory of states (ahwal), which divides things into four categories. Reflect on how this theory supports the classification.
The four categories are existents, non-existents, and the two intermediary states (though only two are mentioned).

(قوله: عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً تُطْلَقُ الصّفَةُ على : المعنى الوجودي القائم بالموصوف، :وعلى ما ليس بذاتٍ، وهذا هو المراد هنا؛ لأن هذه العشرين منها ما هو وجودي كالقُدْرَة والإرادةِ، ومنها ما هو حال كالكَوْنِ قادراً والكونِ مُرِيداً، ومنها ما هو عَدَمِيٌّ كالقِدَمِ والبقاء، وما ذَكَرَهُ المصنف من أن الواجب التفصيلي عشرون صفةً، والمستحيل التفصيلي كذلك؛ مبني على القول بثبوت الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء أربعة أقسام موجودات وهي ما تَصِحُ رُؤْيَتُهُ، ومعدومات: ما لا تُبُوتَ له.

This passage defines the term 'sifa' as either an existential meaning subsisting in the described or what is not a self (i.e., a state or relation). It notes that this classification is based on the theory of states (ahwal) which divides things into four categories. This sets up the next discussion.

Qiyam al-Sifa as Hal Nafsi for Attribute
Clarification that qiyam al-sifa is a hal nafsi for the attribute but amr i'tibari for the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how qiyam al-sifa is a hal nafsi for the attribute itself while being an amr i'tibari for the essence.

Core Idea
The subsistence of an attribute is a real state for the attribute, but a mental consideration for the essence.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies a subtle point about the ontological status of attributes subsisting in a subject. The concept of qiyam al-sifa is often misunderstood as merely a mental consideration. However, the author explains that relative to the attribute, it is an intrinsic state, while relative to the essence, it is a consideration. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the shift in perspective between essence and attribute.
2
Identify the two perspectives
Notice that the passage distinguishes between 'with respect to the essence' and 'with respect to the attribute itself'. Each yields a different classification.
3
Understand why it is a hal nafsi for the attribute
The passage explains that existence of anything (essence or attribute) is an intrinsic state for that thing. Since qiyam is the existence of the attribute in the subject, it is a real state for the attribute.
The term 'hal nafsi' means a real, non-mental state.
4
Reflect on the authorities and difficulty
The author cites two commentaries to support his point and notes the topic's difficulty. Consider why such a subtle distinction matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف أمرٌ اعتباري لا حال هو بالنسبة إلى الذات، وأما بالنسبة إلى الصفة نفسها؛ فهو حال نفسي لها؛ لأنه عبارة عن وجودها في الموصوف، ووجود الشيء سواء كان ذاتاً أو صفةً حال نفسي له كما يعلم ذلك من حاشية الهدهدي وحاشية الدسوقي على المصنف. ففيهما التصريح بأن المراد بالذات في قولهم الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات الشيء ذاتاً كان أو صفةً وبأن قيام العرض بالذات حال نفسي للعرض، فتلخص أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف حال نفسي بالنسبة إلى الصفة لكونه قاراً لها، وأمر اعتباري بالنسبة إلى الذات لكونه ليس قاراً لها، بل لصفتها، وإنما أطلنا الكلام في هذا المقام لصعوبته.
And what was mentioned, that the subsistence (qiyām) of the attribute by the subject (al-mawṣūf) is a consideration (amr iʿtibārī) and not a state (ḥāl), is [so] with respect to the essence (al-dhāt). But with respect to the attribute itself, it is an intrinsic state (ḥāl nafsī) for it, because it is an expression of its existence (wujūd) in the subject. And the existence of a thing, whether it be an essence or an attribute, is an intrinsic state for it, as is known from the gloss of al-Hadiddī and the gloss of al-Dasūqī on the text (al-muṣannaf). For in these two there is explicit statement that by 'essence' (al-dhāt) in their statement 'the state necessary for the essence as long as the essence' is meant the thing, whether it be an essence or an attribute, and that the subsistence of an accident (ʿaraḍ) by the essence is an intrinsic state for the accident. So it is summarized that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is an intrinsic state with respect to the attribute because it is stable (qārrun) for it, and a consideration with respect to the essence because it is not stable for it, but rather for its attribute. And we have only lengthened speech on this topic because of its difficulty.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides a crucial clarification about the nature of 'qiyam al-sifa bi al-mawṣūf' (the subsistence of an attribute in its subject). It is described as an 'amr i'tibari' but not a 'hal' (state) when considered in relation to the essence (dhāt). However, when considered in relation to the attribute itself, it is an 'intrinsic state' (hal nafsi) for the attribute, because it is nothing other than the existence of the attribute in the subject. The existence of any thing, whether essence or attribute, is an intrinsic state for that thing. The author cites two authoritative commentaries (Hāshiyat al-Hadiddī and Hāshiyat al-Dasūqī) which clarify that in the phrase 'the state necessary for the essence', the term 'essence' refers to any thing, whether it be an essence or an attribute. The subsistence of an accident (the attribute considered as accident) in the subject is an intrinsic state for that accident. Thus, the conclusion is that the subsistence of the attribute by the subject is an intrinsic state relative to the attribute because it is stable (qārrun) for it, but it is a consideration (amr i'tibari) relative to the essence because it is not stable for the essence, but rather for its attribute. The author notes the difficulty of this topic, hence the extended discussion.

Key Terms
القيام (al-qiyām)
الصفة (al-ṣifa)
الموصوف (al-mawṣūf)
الحال النفسي (al-ḥāl al-nafsī)
الأمر الاعتباري (al-amr al-iʿtibārī)
العرض (al-ʿaraḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qiyam al-sifa is merely a mental consideration with no real ontological status.
Correction: While qiyam al-sifa is a 'consideration' relative to the essence, it is a real intrinsic state (hal nafsi) for the attribute itself, because it is the existence of the attribute in the subject.

Checkpoint
What is qiyam al-sifa bi al-mawṣūf with respect to the attribute itself?
A) An amr i'tibari
B) A hal nafsi
C) A mere mental concept
D) A state for the essence

Key Takeaway
Qiyam al-sifa is an intrinsic state for the attribute itself, but a mental consideration for the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attribute of 'Knowledge', do you typically conceive of it as something 'added' to His essence, or as His essence itself? This lesson shows that the attribute's subsistence in the essence is a mental consideration for the essence, but a real state for the attribute. How does this affect your understanding of the reality of God's attributes? Can you humbly accept that your mind cannot fully grasp how these attributes relate to His essence?
?
If the existence of an attribute is an intrinsic state for that attribute, but only a mental consideration for the essence, then our knowledge of God's attributes is partly dependent on our conceptualization. Does this humble you? How will you guard against reducing God's attributes to mere human concepts, while still affirming their reality?
?
The author states that he lengthened the speech because of the topic's difficulty. When you encounter a complex truth about God that your mind struggles with, do you tend to give up or do you persist? What specific step can you take this week to deepen your understanding of God's attributes, even if it requires effort?

The Preponderant View: Negation of States
Statement of the preponderant view negating states and its basis in a threefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preponderant view regarding the negation of states (al-aḥwāl) and why it relies on a threefold division of existence.

Core Idea
The negation of states does not eliminate all intermediate concepts; it reframes them as mere considerations.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary presents the preponderant (rajih) position in the classical debate over 'states' (ahwal). The view that states are negated is built on a fundamental division of things into only three categories: essence, attribute, and rational consideration. Understanding this tripartite division is essential for grasping the proper approach to divine attributes in Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'الأشياء ثلاثة أقسام فقط'.
2
Identify the preponderant view
Notice that the passage says 'وهذه الطريقةُ هي الراجحةُ' – this method is the preponderant. Understand that this method negates states.
The passage contrasts this with the view of some verifiers who say 'لا حال' and 'الحال محال'.
3
Reflect on the nuance
The passage acknowledges that the issue is well-known for disagreement and that each view has its proofs. Ask yourself: why does the author still call one view preponderant?
The final 'فتدبر' (so reflect) invites deeper contemplation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا على القول بنفي الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء ثلاثة أقسام فقط، وهذه الطريقةُ هي الراجحَةُ. بل قال بعضُ المحققين الحقُّ أن لا حَالَ وأن الحالَ مُحَالٌ، لكن قال في بعض كتبه وبالجملة، فالمسألة مشهورة الخلاف. ولكلّ من القولين أَدِلَّةٌ تُعْلَمُ من محلّها. فتدبر.
No, upon the view negating states (al-qawl bi nafy al-aḥwāl) based on the method that says that things are only three divisions, and this method is the preponderant (al-rājiḥa). Rather, some of the verifiers said: the truth is that there is no state (lā ḥāla) and that the state is impossible (muḥāl). But he said in some of his books: and in brief, the issue is well-known in disagreement. And for each of the two views there are proofs known from their places. So reflect.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the preponderant (rajih) view in the debate over states (ahwal). It affirms that the correct position is the negation of states, which is built on the method that divides things into only three categories: essence, attribute, and the consideration (amr i'tibari). This tripartite division is considered the strongest. The passage then quotes some verifiers (muhaqqiqun) who assert that there is no state and that a state is impossible (muḥāl). However, it notes that even among those who hold this view, there is nuance: in some of his books, an author might still acknowledge that the issue is well-known for its disagreement. Each of the two views (affirming or negating states) has its own proofs, which are to be studied in their proper places. The final 'fa-tadabbar' (so reflect) invites the reader to contemplate the matter.

Key Terms
الأحوال (al-aḥwāl)
القول بنفي الأحوال (al-qawl bi nafy al-aḥwāl)
الأقسام الثلاثة (al-aqsām al-thalātha)
الراجحة (al-rājiḥa)
المحال (al-muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the negation of states means absolute denial of any intermediate between essence and attribute.
Correction: The passage shows that the tripartite division includes a third category (amr i'tibari), which is a rational consideration, not a separate state. So the negation of states does not eliminate all intermediates; it reclassifies them.

Checkpoint
How many divisions does the rajih method consider for things?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Indefinite

Key Takeaway
The preponderant view negates separate states, asserting that all existents fall into three categories: essence, attribute, and rational consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the preponderant view divides all things into only three categories. When you reflect on Allah's attributes, are you tempted to imagine a 'state' between essence and attribute that you cannot define? What must you do to align your thinking with this precise division? Will you accept that your mind's conception of a 'state' may be a mere rational consideration, and not a real entity?
?
The passage ends with 'so reflect.' Take a moment: in your own study of divine attributes, have you ever assumed that your reasoning about 'states' was correct without checking it against the established threefold division? What specific step will you take today to ensure your understanding of Allah's attributes is built on sound theological principles rather than your own assumptions?
?
Some verifiers went as far as to say 'the state is impossible.' Does your heart find that hard to accept? Why? Is it because you feel that without 'states' something is missing in your understanding of how attributes relate to Allah's essence? Confront that discomfort: are you willing to submit to the truth even if it challenges your mental models? What will you do with the intellectual humility this demands?
