The Divine Will: Definition and Its Attachments
Al-Bajuri defines the divine will and distinguishes its two executive attachments from the pre-dispositive one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine will as an attribute that specifies the possible, and the distinction between its executive attachments (eternal and originated) and the pre-dispositive attachment that it lacks.

Core Idea
Unlike power, the divine will has only executive attachments, not a pre-dispositive one.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the attribute of will (al-irāda) as defined by the mutakallimūn. He then clarifies a key nuance: the will's specification of possibilities is always actual, never merely potential. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms and the flow of the definition.
2
Identify the definition and the key term 'specifies'
The passage defines will as an attribute that 'specifies' (tukhaṣṣiṣu) the possible. Note that this specification is the core function of the will.
The term 'takhṣīṣ' is central to understanding the attachments.
3
Distinguish the three attachments
The passage describes two executive attachments (eternal and originated) and explicitly excludes a pre-dispositive attachment. Understand why the pre-dispositive is not applicable to will.
This distinction is crucial for differentiating will from power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
:(قوله: (والإرادة) هى صِفَةٌ وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى تُخَصِّصُ الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه. كذا قال المتكلمون وفي قولهم تُخَصِصُ الممكن... إلخ إشارة إلى تعلقها التنجيزي القديم، وهو تخصيص شيء ببعض ما يجوز عليه أَزَلاً، أو إلى تعلقها التنجيزي الحادِثِ بناءً على القول به، وهو تخصيص الشيء بذلك حين إيجاده وإعدامه، لا إلى تعلقه الصُّلُوحِيّ القديم، وهو صلاحيتها أزلاً لتخصيص الممكن بكلّ شيءٍ مما جاز عليه .
His statement: 'And the will (al-irāda) is an existing attribute subsisting in His essence, may He be exalted, that specifies the possible (al-mumkin) with some of what is permissible for it.' Thus said the mutakallimūn. And in their statement 'specifies the possible... etc.' there is an indication to its temporal executive attachment which is eternal (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm), which is the specification of a thing with some of what is permissible for it eternally, or to its temporal executive attachment which is originated (al-ḥādith) based on the affirmation of it, which is the specification of the thing with that at the time of its bringing into existence and causing its nonexistence, not to its pre-dispositive eternal attachment (al-ta'alluq al-ṣulūḥī al-qadīm), which is its suitability eternally for specifying the possible with every thing from what is permissible for it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of will (al-irāda) from al-Bajuri's commentary. It begins by quoting Sanusi's matn: 'Will is an existing attribute subsisting in God's essence, that specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it.' The mutakallimun (theologians) have defined it thus. Al-Bajuri then explains that the phrase 'specifies the possible...' indicates one of two types of attachment: either the eternal temporal executive attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm), which is God's eternal specification of a thing from pre-eternity, or the originated temporal executive attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-ḥādith), which is the specification at the moment of bringing into existence or causing nonexistence (this latter is based on the acceptance of a originated attachment). Importantly, the definition does NOT refer to a pre-dispositive eternal attachment (al-ta'alluq al-ṣulūḥī al-qadīm), which would be the will's eternal suitability to specify the possible with any of its permissible states. Thus, unlike power, will does not have a purely potential pre-dispositive attachment; rather, its attachments are always executive, either eternal (pre‑eternal decree) or temporal (actual specification at the time of action).

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irāda)
التخصيص (al-takhṣīṣ)
التعلق التنجيزي القديم (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm)
التعلق التنجيزي الحادث (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-ḥādith)
التعلق الصلوحي القديم (al-ta'alluq al-ṣulūḥī al-qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that will, like power, has a pre-dispositive eternal attachment (i.e., a potential to specify without actually specifying).
Correction: The passage explicitly denies this: will only has executive attachments (eternal or originated). The will's specification is always actual, never merely potential.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what types of attachment does the divine will have?
A) Only a pre-dispositive eternal attachment.
B) Only a temporal executive attachment.
C) Two executive attachments (eternal and originated) and no pre-dispositive attachment.
D) Both a pre-dispositive and a temporal executive attachment.

Key Takeaway
The divine will specifies possibilities perpetually and actually; it has no merely potential (pre-dispositive) attachment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's will is always actually specifying every possible thing, what does that mean for the events in your life that you consider random or insignificant? How should this change your trust in His planning?
?
Knowing that the divine will has no mere potential—it always determines a specific outcome—how does that confront your own hesitation and indecision? What area of your life do you need to submit more fully to His actual will?
?
The passage teaches that Allah's will is eternally specifying some possibilities and not others. Are there desires you hold onto that you know contradict His specified will? What would it look like to surrender them today, not just intellectually but in your heart's attachment?

The Attachments of the Divine Will: Three or Two?
Al-Bajuri discusses the number of attachments of the divine will and the metaphorical attribution of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over the number of attachments of the divine will and why attributing specification to attributes is a mental metaphor.

Core Idea
The will's attachments are two, not three — and all action belongs to Allah alone.

This passage from Al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies a key debate in Ash'ari theology: how many attachments does the divine will have? He shows that while some hold there are three, the verified position is two, and explains why attributing specification to the will or effect to power is metaphorical — the real doer is Allah's Essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the flow from discussing 'specification' to concluding about attachments.
There is a footnote marker (۱) in the Arabic.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the statement about three attachments based on temporal actualizing attachment, and then the verification that there are only two.
3
Understand the metaphor explanation
Al-Bajuri explains that attributing specification to the will and effect to power is mental metaphor. Ask why he says this.
He connects it to the principle that only Allah's Essence truly acts.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider how this preserves tawhid by not assigning independent creative power to attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن المتبادِرَ من التعبير بالتخصيص أن المراد التخصيص بالفعل، وأيضاً التعبير بـ: بعض ما يجوز عليه يقتضيه؛ لأنها تَصْلُحُ في الأزل لتخصيص الممكن بكلّ شيءٍ مما جاز عليه لا بالبعض فقط . فتلخص أن للإرادة ثلاثة (۱) تعلقات بناءً على القول بأن لها تعلقاً تنجيزياً حادثاً. والتحقيق: أن ذلك ليس تعلقاً مستقلاً، بل إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، وعلى هذا فيكون لها تعلُّقان فقط: أحدهما: صلوحي قديم، والآخرُ: تنجيزي قديم، وإسنادُ التخصيص إليها مجاز عقلي من الإسناد إلى السبب، وإلا؛ فالمخصص حقيقة هو الذاتُ الأَقْدَسُ، وكذلك إسنادُ التأثير إلى القدرة في قول بعضهم: هي صفةٌ تُؤَثِرُ في الممكن الوجود أو العدم، فهو مجاز عقلي من باب الإسناد إلى السبب، وإلا فالمؤثِرُ حقيقةً هو الذاتُ الأقدسُ ؛ إذ لا فِعْلَ إلا له كما نص عليه غيرُ واحد من المحققين.
Because the common understanding from the expression 'specification' is that what is meant is specification by action, and also the expression 'some of what is permissible upon Him' necessitates it because it is suitable in pre-eternity for specifying the possible with everything that is permissible upon it, not only some. So it follows that the will has three attachments based on the statement that it has a temporal actualizing attachment. But the verification is that this is not an independent attachment, rather it is a manifestation of the eternal actualizing attachment; and on this basis it has only two attachments: one is an eternal potential attachment, and the other is an eternal actualizing attachment. Attributing specification to it is a mental metaphor of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the real specifier is the Most Holy Essence. Likewise, attributing effect to power in the statement of some: 'It is an attribute that effects existence or non-existence in the possible' is a mental metaphor of the type of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the real efficient is the Most Holy Essence, for there is no act except His, as more than one of the verifying scholars have stated.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage by Al-Bajuri is an analytical explanation of the term 'specification' (takhsis) in relation to the divine will. He begins by noting that the common understanding of 'specification' implies an action, and that the expression 'some of what is permissible upon Him' necessitates that the will is suitable in pre-eternity for specifying any possible with any aspect that is permissible, not just some aspects. From this, he concludes that the will has three attachments if one holds that it has a temporal actualizing attachment (ta'alluq tanjizi hadith). However, the verified truth (tahqiq) is that this temporal attachment is not independent; rather, it is a manifestation of the eternal actualizing attachment. Thus, there are only two attachments: an eternal potential attachment and an eternal actualizing attachment. Then Al-Bajuri clarifies that attributing specification to the will is a mental metaphor, because the real specifier is the divine Essence. Similarly, attributing effect (ta'thir) to God's power (qudra) is also a mental metaphor, because the real agent is the Essence alone, as established by the verifying scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine will or power independently causes specification or effect without Allah's Essence.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that attributing action to attributes is metaphorical; the real cause is the Most Holy Essence.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, why is attributing specification to the divine will considered a mental metaphor?
A) Because the will is not a real attribute
B) Because the real specifier is the divine Essence
C) Because the will only has potential attachments
D) Because specification occurs only in time

Key Takeaway
The divine will has only two attachments, and all agency belongs solely to Allah's Essence — attributing actions to attributes is merely metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something in life, do you genuinely believe that the 'real cause' is the Most Holy Essence, or do you feel as though your skills or willpower were the true agents? What would it look like to live in radical reliance on Allah alone, attributing every success and failure directly to Him?
?
If the act of attributing effect to power is a mental metaphor, then what is the reality of your own power? How does this shift your understanding of personal responsibility and dependence on Allah?
?
This lesson argues that even the will's specification is metaphorically attributed to the will. Does that confront you with the fact that every 'decision' you think you make is ultimately from Allah? How can you cultivate a heart that sees the Hand of Allah behind every ‘cause’ and yet does not neglect the means?
